$$F\_p^2\left(x\right) = f\_p\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla f\_p\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2}\eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 f\_p\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right), p \in \left\{1, 2\right\}, \eta$$

*while first approximate functions for the constraint is:*

$$G\_t^1\left(x\right) = g\_t\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla g\_t\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right), t \in \left\{1, 2, 3\right\}.$$

*Considering* η (x, x0) = x − x<sup>0</sup> *we get:*

$$\begin{aligned} F\_1^i \left( x \right) &= F\_1^i \left( x \right) = x\_1 - 2x\_2, \; i \in \{0, 1, 2\} \\\\ F\_2^j \left( x \right) &= F\_2^j \left( x \right) = x\_1 + x\_2, \; j \in \{0, 1, 2\} \end{aligned}$$

*and*

$$G\_1^1(x) = -x\_1 - x\_2 + 2,\ G\_2^1(x) = -x\_1,\ G\_3^1(x) = -x\_2$$

*Consequently, the approximate problems* P i,j 1 *, with* (i, j) ∈ {(1, 0),(1, 1), (2, 0),(2, 1),(2, 2)} *are:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( x\_1 - 2x\_2; x\_1 + x\_2 \right) \\ -x\_1 - x\_2 + 2 \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*Calculating the value of objective function for problem* P i,j 1 *in* x = (0, 2) ∈ F <sup>1</sup> *we obtain:*

$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^i, F\_2^j\right)(0, \, 2) &= \left(-4, \, \, 2\right) \le \left(-1, \, \, 2\right) = \left(F\_1^i, F\_2^j\right)(1, \, 1) \\ \left(F\_1^i, F\_2^j\right)(0, \, 2) &\ne \left(F\_1^i, F\_2^j\right)(1, \, 1) \end{aligned}$$

*where* (i, j) ∈ {(1, 0), (1, 1), (2, 0), (2, 1), (2, 2)}*, which proves that* x<sup>0</sup> = (1, 1) ∈ F<sup>1</sup> *is not an efficient solution for approximate problem* P i,j 1 *.*

### <span id="page-50-0"></span>**Example 3.4.12 (Luca and Duca [\[95\]](#page-180-2))** *.*

*Let the initial bi-criteria optimization problem* P 0,0 0 *be:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( x\_1^2 + (x\_2 - \pi - 1)^2; \ (x\_1 + \frac{1}{10})^2 - \frac{1}{2}(x\_2 + 1)^2 \right) \\ \quad - x\_1 - \sin x\_1 + x\_2 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*An efficient solution of problem* P 0,0 0 *is* x<sup>0</sup> = (<sup>π</sup> 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *and the value of the objective function in* x<sup>0</sup> *is* f( π 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) = ( <sup>π</sup> 2 2 ; π 2 <sup>8</sup> − 9π <sup>10</sup> − 199 <sup>100</sup> )*.* *To compute the approximate problem* P 1,1 1 *in* x<sup>0</sup> *we have to calculate:*

$$F\_p^1\left(x\right) = f\_p\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla f\_p\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \; p \in \{1, 2\}.$$

*and*

$$G\_t^1\left(x\right) = g\_t\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla g\_t\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \; t \in \left\{1, 2, 3, 4\right\}.$$

*Considering* η (x, x0) = x − x<sup>0</sup> *we get:*

$$F\_1^1\left(x\right) = \pi x\_1 - \pi x\_2 + \pi + \frac{\pi^2}{2},$$

$$F\_2^1(x) = (\pi + \frac{1}{5})x\_1 - (\frac{\pi}{2} + 2)x\_2 - \frac{\pi^2}{8} + \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{1}{100},$$

G 1 1 (x) = −x<sup>1</sup> +x<sup>2</sup> −1, G<sup>1</sup> 2 (x) = x<sup>1</sup> − 5π 2 , G<sup>1</sup> 3 (x) = −x1, G<sup>1</sup> 4 (x) = −x2.

*Thus, the approximate problem* P 1,1 1 *is:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( \pi x\_1 - \pi x\_2 + \pi + \frac{\pi^2}{2}; \left( \pi + \frac{1}{5} \right) x\_1 - \left( \frac{\pi}{2} + 2 \right) x\_2 - \frac{\pi^2}{8} + \frac{\pi}{2} + \frac{1}{100} \right) \\\quad - x\_1 + x\_2 - 1 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*Calculating the value for the objective function of problem* P 1,1 1 *in* x = ( 5π 2 , 1 + <sup>5</sup><sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>1</sup> *we get:*

$$\begin{array}{c} F^1(\frac{5\pi}{2}, 1 + \frac{5\pi}{2}) = (\frac{\pi^2}{2}, \frac{9\pi^2}{8} - \frac{9\pi}{2} - \frac{199}{100}) \\ \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \leq (\frac{\pi^2}{2}, \frac{\pi^2}{8} - \frac{9\pi}{10} - \frac{199}{100}) \\ \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \qquad \quad \frac{\pi}{2}(1 + \frac{\pi}{2}) \\ F^1(\frac{5\pi}{2}, 1 + \frac{5\pi}{2}) \neq F^1(\frac{\pi}{2}, 1 + \frac{\pi}{2}) \end{array}$$

*and thus we have proved that* x<sup>0</sup> = (<sup>π</sup> 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) *is not an efficient solution for problem* P 1,1 1 *.*

## <span id="page-52-1"></span>**3.5 First and second** η **- approximations for components of objective function on the second** η **- approximation of feasible set**

In this section we will study conditions such that efficient solutions of approximated problems P 1,0 2 , P 2,0 2 , P 2,1 2 and P 2,2 2 will remain efficient also for original problem P 0,0 0 and reciprocally.

Case P 1,1 2 was studied in [\[12\]](#page-172-9), where also conditions such that F <sup>0</sup> ⊆ F<sup>2</sup> and F <sup>2</sup> ⊆ F<sup>0</sup> were analyzed. We will use them in our work, so we will briefly present the Theorems stating these inclusions.

### **Theorem 3.5.1 (Duca and Boncea [\[12\]](#page-172-9))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*, and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S)*.*

*Assume that:*

- **a)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **b)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*

*then*

$$
\mathcal{F}^0 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^2.
$$

### **Theorem 3.5.2 (Duca and Boncea [\[12\]](#page-172-9))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*, and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S)*.*

*Assume that*

- **a)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **b)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*

<span id="page-52-0"></span>*then*

$$
\mathcal{F}^2 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^0.
$$

### **Theorem 3.5.3 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

*Assume that:*

**a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*

- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **f)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

*If* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 2,1 2 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 0,0 0 *.*

**Proof.** x<sup>0</sup> being an efficient solution for P 2,1 2 , implies that @x ∈ F 2 s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^2\left(x\right), F\_2^1\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(F\_1^2\left(x\right), F\_2^1\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) .\end{aligned}$$

Conditions b) and c) imply that

F <sup>0</sup> ⊆ F<sup>2</sup>

and thus @x ∈ F<sup>0</sup> s.t.

<span id="page-53-1"></span>
$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^2\left(x\right), F\_2^1\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(F\_1^2\left(x\right), F\_2^1\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) . \end{aligned} \tag{3.15}$$

Let's assume that x<sup>0</sup> is not an efficient solution for P 0,0 0 . Then ∃y ∈ F 0 s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(f\_1\left(y\right), f\_2\left(y\right)\right) &\leq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right), \\ \left(f\_1\left(y\right), f\_2\left(y\right)\right) &\neq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \end{aligned}$$

which implies that ∃y ∈ F<sup>0</sup> s.t.

<span id="page-53-0"></span>
$$\begin{cases} \quad f\_1\left(y\right) < f\_1\left(x\_0\right) \\ \quad f\_2\left(y\right) \le f\_2\left(x\_0\right) \end{cases} \tag{3.16}$$

<span id="page-54-1"></span>or

$$\begin{cases} \quad f\_1\left(y\right) \le f\_1\left(x\_0\right) \\\quad f\_2\left(y\right) < f\_2\left(x\_0\right). \end{cases} \tag{3.17}$$

Because f<sup>1</sup> is invex<sup>2</sup> at x<sup>0</sup> with respect to η we get F 2 1 (y) ≤ f<sup>1</sup> (y), ∀y ∈ F 0 . Because f<sup>2</sup> is invex<sup>1</sup> at x<sup>0</sup> with respect to η we get F 1 2 (y) ≤ f<sup>2</sup> (y), ∀y ∈ F<sup>0</sup> . Because η (x<sup>0</sup> x0) = 0 we get f<sup>1</sup> (x0) = F 2 1 (x0) and f<sup>2</sup> (x0) = F 1 2 (x0). Thus from [\(3.16\)](#page-53-0) we get that ∃y ∈ F<sup>0</sup> s.t.

$$\begin{cases} \, \, \_1F\_1^2 \left( y \right) < \, F\_1^2 \left( x\_0 \right) \\\, \, \_2F\_2^1 \left( y \right) \le \, F\_2^1 \left( x\_0 \right) \end{cases}$$

which contradicts [\(3.15\)](#page-53-1) and from [\(3.17\)](#page-54-1) we get that ∃y ∈ F<sup>0</sup> s.t.

$$\begin{cases} \, \, \_1F\_1^2\left(y\right) \le \, \_1F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right) \\\, \, \_2F\_2^1\left(y\right) < \, \_2F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right) \end{cases}$$

which contradicts [\(3.15\)](#page-53-1).

<span id="page-54-0"></span>In conclusion x<sup>0</sup> is an efficient solution for P 0,0 0 .

### **Theorem 3.5.4 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

*Assume that:*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **f)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

*If* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 0,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 2,1 2 *.*

**Proof.** x<sup>0</sup> being an efficient solution for P 0,0 0 , implies that @x ∈ F 0 s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(f\_1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(f\_1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \dots \end{aligned}$$

Conditions b) and c) imply that

$$\mathcal{F}^2 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^0$$

and thus @x ∈ F<sup>2</sup> s.t.

<span id="page-55-2"></span>
$$\begin{aligned} \left(f\_1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(f\_1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(f\_1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) . \end{aligned} \tag{3.18}$$

Let's assume that x<sup>0</sup> is not an efficient solution for P 2,1 2 . Then ∃y ∈ F 2 s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^2\left(y\right), F\_2^1\left(y\right)\right) &\leq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(F\_1^2\left(y\right), F\_2^1\left(y\right)\right) &\neq \left(F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right), F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right)\right) \end{aligned}$$

which implies that ∃y ∈ F<sup>2</sup> s.t.

<span id="page-55-1"></span>
$$\begin{cases} F\_1^2(y) < F\_1^2(x\_0) \\\ F\_2^1(y) \le F\_2^1(x\_0) \end{cases} \tag{3.19}$$

<span id="page-55-3"></span>or

$$\begin{cases} \quad F\_1^2\left(y\right) \le F\_1^2\left(x\_0\right) \\\ F\_2^1\left(y\right) < F\_2^1\left(x\_0\right). \end{cases} \tag{3.20}$$

Because f<sup>1</sup> is incave<sup>2</sup> at x<sup>0</sup> with respect to η we get f<sup>1</sup> (y) ≤ F 2 1 (y), ∀y ∈ F 2 . Because f<sup>2</sup> is incave<sup>1</sup> at x<sup>0</sup> with respect to η we get f<sup>2</sup> (y) ≤ F 1 2 (y), ∀y ∈ F<sup>2</sup> . Because η (x<sup>0</sup> x0) = 0 we get f<sup>1</sup> (x0) = F 2 1 (x0) and f<sup>2</sup> (x0) = F 1 2 (x0). Thus from [\(3.19\)](#page-55-1) we get that ∃y ∈ F<sup>2</sup> s.t.

$$\begin{cases} \, \, f\_1 \left( y \right) < f\_1 \left( x\_0 \right) \\ \, \, f\_2 \left( y \right) \le f\_2 \left( x\_0 \right) \end{cases}$$

which contradicts [\(3.18\)](#page-55-2) and from [\(3.20\)](#page-55-3) we get that ∃y ∈ F<sup>2</sup> s.t.

$$\begin{cases} \, \, f\_1 \left( y \right) \le f\_1 \left( x\_0 \right) \\\, \, f\_2 \left( y \right) < f\_2 \left( x\_0 \right) \end{cases}$$

which contradicts [\(3.18\)](#page-55-2).

<span id="page-55-0"></span>In conclusion x<sup>0</sup> is an efficient solution for P 2,1 2 .

### **Theorem 3.5.5 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions. Assume that:*

**a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*

- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

*If* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 1,0 2 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 0,0 0 *.*

<span id="page-56-0"></span>**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.5.3](#page-52-0)*.

### **Theorem 3.5.6 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions. Assume that:*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>1</sup> *at* x0*with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

*If* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 0,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 1,0 2 *.*

<span id="page-56-1"></span>**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.5.4](#page-54-0)*.

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*

### **Theorem 3.5.8 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*

### **Theorem 3.5.9 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions. Assume that:*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **f)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

### **Theorem 3.5.10 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **d)** f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **e)** f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **f)** η (x0, x0) = 0*.*

*If* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 0,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for* P 2,2 2 *.*

<span id="page-59-0"></span>**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.5.4](#page-54-0)*.

### **Example 3.5.11 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let the initial bi-criteria optimization problem* P 0,0 0 *be:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( -\left( x\_1 - \frac{3\pi}{5} \right)^2 - \left( x\_2 - \frac{2\pi}{5} - 1 \right)^2; -x\_1 + x\_2 \right) \\ \quad - x\_1 - \sin x\_1 + x\_2 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*An efficient solution of problem* P 0,0 0 *is* x<sup>0</sup> = (<sup>π</sup> 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *. Second order approximate functions for the constraints are:*

$$G\_t^2\left(x\right) = g\_t\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla g\_t\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2}\eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 g\_t\eta\left(x, x\_0\right), t \in \{1, 2, 3, 4\}$$

*Considering* η (x, x0) = x − x<sup>0</sup> *we get:*

$$\begin{aligned} G\_1^2(x) &= -x\_1 + x\_2 + \frac{1}{2} \left( x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - 1, \\\\ G\_2^2(x) &= x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2}, \; G\_3^2(x) = -x\_1, \; G\_4^2(x) = -x\_2. \end{aligned}$$

*Consequently, the approximate problem* P 0,0 2 *is:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( -\left( x\_1 - \frac{3\pi}{5} \right)^2 - \left( x\_2 - \frac{2\pi}{5} - 1 \right)^2; -x\_1 + x\_2 \right) \\\ -x\_1 + x\_2 + \frac{1}{2} \left( x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - 1 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*Calculating the values of objective function for problem* P 0,0 2 *in* x<sup>0</sup> = ( π 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *and* x = ( <sup>3</sup><sup>π</sup> 4 ; 3π <sup>4</sup> + 1 − π 2 <sup>32</sup> ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *we obtain:*

$$f(\frac{3\pi}{4}; \frac{3\pi}{4} + 1 - \frac{\pi^2}{32}) = (-\frac{58\pi^2}{400} + \frac{14\pi^3}{640} - \frac{\pi^4}{32}); \ 1 - \frac{\pi^2}{32})$$

*and*

$$f(\frac{\pi}{2}, \ 1 + \frac{\pi}{2}) = (-\frac{\pi^2}{50}, \ 1).$$

<span id="page-59-1"></span>*Because* (− 58π 2 <sup>400</sup> + 14π 3 <sup>640</sup> − π 4 <sup>32</sup> ; 1 − π 2 <sup>32</sup> ) < (− π 2 <sup>50</sup> , 1) *it follows that* x<sup>0</sup> = ( π 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) *is not an efficient solution for approximate problem* P 0,0 2 *.*

**Example 3.5.12** *Let's consider the same initial problem as in Example [3.5.11.](#page-59-0) First order approximations for the components of the objective function are*

$$F\_p^1\left(x\right) = f\_p\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla f\_p\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \; p \in \left\{1, 2\right\}.$$

*Considering* η (x, x0) = x − x<sup>0</sup> *we get:*

$$F\_1^1\left(x\right) = -\frac{\pi}{5}x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{5}x\_2 + \frac{9\pi^2}{50} + \frac{\pi}{5}$$

*and*

$$F\_2^1\left(x\right) = -x\_1 + x\_2.$$

*Approximate functions for the constrains are the same computed at Example [3.5.11.](#page-59-0) Consequently the approximate problem* P 1,1 2 *is:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( -\frac{\pi}{5}x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{5}x\_2 + \frac{9\pi^2}{50} + \frac{\pi}{5}; -x\_1 + x\_2 \right) \\ \quad - x\_1 + x\_2 + \frac{1}{2} \left( x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - 1 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*Calculating the values for the objective function of problem* P 1,1 2 *in* x<sup>0</sup> = ( π 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *and in* x = ( <sup>3</sup><sup>π</sup> 4 ; 3π <sup>4</sup> + 1 − π 2 <sup>32</sup> ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *we get that*

$$F^1(\frac{3\pi}{4}; \frac{3\pi}{4} + 1 - \frac{\pi^2}{32}) < F^1(\frac{\pi}{2}, \, 1 + \frac{\pi}{2})$$

*which proves that* x<sup>0</sup> = ( <sup>π</sup> 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) *is not an efficient solution for problem* P 1,1 2 *.*

### <span id="page-60-0"></span>**Example 3.5.13 (Luca and Duca [\[94\]](#page-179-2))** *.*

*Let's consider the same initial problem as in Example [3.5.11.](#page-59-0) Second order approximations for the components of the objective function are*

$$F\_p^2\left(x\right) = f\_p\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla f\_p\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2}\eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 f\_p\left(x\_0\right)\eta\left(x, x\_0\right), p \in \left\{1, 2\right\}.$$

*Considering* η (x, x0) = x − x<sup>0</sup> *we get:*

$$F\_1^2\left(x\right) = -\frac{\pi}{2}\left(x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2}\right)^2 - \frac{\pi + 2}{2}\left(x\_2 - 1 - \frac{\pi}{2}\right)^2 - \frac{\pi}{5}x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{5}x\_2 + \frac{9\pi^2}{50} + \frac{\pi}{5}$$

*and*

$$F\_2^2\left(x\right) = -x\_1 + x\_2.$$

*Approximate functions for the constrains are the same computed at Example [3.5.11.](#page-59-0) Consequently the approximate problem* P 2,2 2 *is:*

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( -\frac{\pi}{2} \left( x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - \frac{\pi + 2}{2} \left( x\_2 - 1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - \frac{\pi}{5} x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{5} x\_2 + \frac{9\pi^2}{50} + \frac{\pi}{5}; -x\_1 + x\_2 \right) \\\ -x\_1 + x\_2 + \frac{1}{2} \left( x\_1 - \frac{\pi}{2} \right)^2 - 1 \le 0 \\\ x\_1 - \frac{5\pi}{2} \le 0 \\\ x\_1; x\_2 \ge 0. \end{cases}$$

*Calculating the values for the objective function of problem* P 2,2 2 *in* x<sup>0</sup> = ( π 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *and in* x = ( <sup>3</sup><sup>π</sup> 4 ; 3π <sup>4</sup> + 1 − π 2 <sup>32</sup> ) ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *we get that*

$$F^2(\frac{3\pi}{4}; \frac{3\pi}{4} + 1 - \frac{\pi^2}{32}) < F^2(\frac{\pi}{2}, \ 1 + \frac{\pi}{2})$$

*which proves that* x<sup>0</sup> = ( <sup>π</sup> 2 , 1 + <sup>π</sup> 2 ) *is not an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 2 *.*

### <span id="page-61-1"></span>**3.6 Relations between** η **- approximation problems for** P 0,0 0

After establishing conditions such that efficient solutions of P i,j k remain efficient solutions for P 0,0 0 and reciprocally, it is interesting to study relations between different order η - approximation problems.

We will split this part in three subsection which will study relations between P i,j 0 vs P i,j 1 , P i,j 0 vs P i,j 2 and P i,j 1 vs P i,j 2 .

### <span id="page-61-2"></span>**3.6.1 Relations between** P i,j 0 **and** P i,j 1

### <span id="page-61-0"></span>**Theorem 3.6.1 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

*Assume that:*

$$\mathbf{a} \mathbf{b} \; x\_0 \in \mathcal{F}^1 \; \iota$$

**b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*

- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 1 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 1 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 1 *.*
	- *4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 1 *.*

**Proof.** x<sup>0</sup> being an efficient solution for P 1,0 0 implies that @x ∈ F 0 s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(F\_1^1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \\ \left(F\_1^1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(F\_1^1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) .\end{aligned}$$

Conditions b) and c) imply that

$$\mathcal{F}^1 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^0$$

which means that @x ∈ F<sup>1</sup> s.t.

$$\begin{aligned} \left(F\_1^1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\leq \left(F\_1^1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right),\\ \left(F\_1^1\left(x\right), f\_2\left(x\right)\right) &\neq \left(F\_1^1\left(x\_0\right), f\_2\left(x\_0\right)\right) \end{aligned}$$

and thus x<sup>0</sup> is an efficient solution for problem P 1,0 1 . Similar logic is used to prove the statements 2, 3 and 4.

### <span id="page-62-0"></span>**Theorem 3.6.2 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>1</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 1 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 0 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 1 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 0 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 1 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 0 *.*
	- *4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 1 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 0 *.*

**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.6.1](#page-61-0)*.

### <span id="page-63-1"></span>**3.6.2 Relations between** P i,j 0 **and** P i,j 2

### <span id="page-63-0"></span>**Theorem 3.6.3 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and incave*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 2 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 2 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 2 *.*

*4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 0 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 2 *.*

<span id="page-64-0"></span>**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.6.1](#page-61-0)*.

### **Theorem 3.6.4 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

*Assume that:*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>0</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and invex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and avex*<sup>2</sup> *at* x<sup>0</sup> *with respect to* η*.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 0 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 0 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 0 *.*
	- *4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 0 *.*

**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.6.1](#page-61-0)*.

### <span id="page-64-2"></span>**3.6.3 Relations between** P i,j 1 **and** P i,j 2

### <span id="page-64-1"></span>**Theorem 3.6.5 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*, and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S)*.*

- **a)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) *is negative semi-definite,*
- **b)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) *is null definite,*

*then*

$$
\mathcal{F}^1 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^2.
$$

**Proof.** Let x ∈ F<sup>1</sup> . Then

$$\begin{cases} \quad G\_t^1 \left( x \right) \le 0, \,\forall t \in T \\\ \quad H\_s^1 \left( x \right) = 0, \,\forall s \in S. \end{cases}$$

We know that

$$\begin{aligned} G\_t^2(x) &= \left. g\_t(x\_0) + \nabla g\_t\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2} \eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 g\_t\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \forall t \in T\\ H\_s^2(x) &= \left. h\_s\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla h\_s\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2} \eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 h\_s\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \forall s \in S. \end{aligned}$$

Because ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) is negative semi-definite, it follows that

$$G\_t^2\left(x\right) \le G\_t^1\left(x\right), \ \forall t \in T.$$

Because ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) is null definite, it follows that

$$H\_s^2\left(x\right) = H\_s^1\left(x\right), \,\forall s \in S.$$

We get

$$\begin{cases} \begin{array}{c} G\_t^2 \left(x\right) \le 0, \,\forall t \in T\\ H\_s^2 \left(x\right) = 0, \,\forall s \in S, \end{array} \end{cases}$$

<span id="page-65-0"></span>which implies that x ∈ F<sup>2</sup> and thus F <sup>1</sup> ⊆ F<sup>2</sup> .

### **Theorem 3.6.6 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*, and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S)*. If*

**a)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) *is positive semi-definite,*

**b)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) *is null definite,*

53

*then*

$$
\mathcal{F}^2 \subseteq \mathcal{F}^1.
$$

**Proof.** Let x ∈ F<sup>2</sup> . Then

$$\begin{cases} \quad G\_t^2\left(x\right) \le 0, \,\forall t \in T\\\quad H\_s^2\left(x\right) = 0, \,\forall s \in S. \end{cases}$$

We know that

$$\begin{cases} G\_t^2(x) &= \left. g\_t(x\_0) + \nabla g\_t\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2} \eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 g\_t\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \forall t \in T\\ H\_s^2(x) &= \left. h\_s\left(x\_0\right) + \nabla h\_s\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right) + \frac{1}{2} \eta\left(x, x\_0\right)^T \nabla^2 h\_s\left(x\_0\right) \eta\left(x, x\_0\right), \forall s \in S. \end{cases}$$

Because ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) is positive semi-definite, it follows that

$$G\_t^1(x) \le G\_t^2(x), \,\forall t \in T.$$

Because ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) is null definite, it follows that

$$H\_s^1\left(x\right) = H\_s^2\left(x\right), \,\forall s \in S.$$

We get

$$\begin{cases} \quad G\_t^1 \left( x \right) \le 0, \,\forall t \in T \\\ H\_s^1 \left( x \right) = 0, \,\forall s \in S, \end{cases}$$

<span id="page-66-0"></span>which implies that x ∈ F<sup>1</sup> and thus F <sup>2</sup> ⊆ F<sup>1</sup> .

### **Theorem 3.6.7 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions.*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>2</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) *is positive semi-definite,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) *is null definite.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 1 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 2 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 1 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 2 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 1 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 2 *.*
	- *4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 1 *, then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 2 *.*
- <span id="page-67-0"></span>**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.6.1](#page-61-0)*.

### **Theorem 3.6.8 (Luca and Duca [\[97\]](#page-180-3))** *.*

*Let* X *be a nonempty set of* R n *,* x<sup>0</sup> *an interior point of* X, η : X × X → X*,* T *and* S *index sets,* f = (f1, f2) : X → R 2 *and* g<sup>t</sup> , h<sup>s</sup> : X → R,(t ∈ T, s ∈ S) *functions. Assume that:*

- **a)** x<sup>0</sup> ∈ F<sup>1</sup> *,*
- **b)** *for each* t ∈ T*, the function* g<sup>t</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup> g<sup>t</sup> (x0) *is negative semi-definite,*
- **c)** *for each* s ∈ S*, the function* h<sup>s</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* ∇<sup>2</sup>h<sup>s</sup> (x0) *is null definite.*
	- *1. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 1,0 1 *.*
	- *2. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,0 1 *.*
	- *3. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,1 1 *.*

*4. If* f<sup>1</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x0*,* f<sup>2</sup> *is twice differentiable at* x<sup>0</sup> *and* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 2 *then* x<sup>0</sup> *is an efficient solution for problem* P 2,2 1 *.*

**Proof.** Proof is similar with *Theorem [3.6.1](#page-61-0)*.

## <span id="page-68-0"></span>**3.7 Conclusions**

This chapter was dedicated to study of some conditions such that efficient solution of a bi-criteria optimization problem remains efficient also for the approximate problem and reciprocally. Practical application of this research is visible in situation when a highly complex bicriteria optimization problem has to be solved. It might be replaced with an approximate problem, easier to be solved and under certain conditions (referring to invexity, incavity or avexity of functions involved) the efficient solution of one problem remains efficient also for the other.

To be noticed that in case of the above mentioned conditions are not satisfied, the efficient solution of one problem might remain efficient for the other one or not.

Our research was divided and presented in three sections, dedicated to different type of approximate problems associated to the initial one and another section dedicated to the study of relations which might be established between different type of approximate problems.

We have studied conditions such that efficient solution of the initial problem P 0,0 0 remains efficient for the following type of approximate problems P i,j 0 , P i,j 1 , P i,j 2 , where (i, j) ∈ {(1, 0),(1, 1),(2, 0), (2, 1),(2, 2)} and reciprocally.

Some counter-examples, showing the importance of "invexity-type conditions" are presented. Our contributions to this Chapter, disseminated in [\[96\]](#page-180-1), [\[95\]](#page-180-2), [\[94\]](#page-179-2) and [\[97\]](#page-180-3) consisting of 32 Theorems and 7 counter-examples, might be synthesized as:

• 8 Theorems for conditions such that efficient solution of problem P 0,0 0 remains efficient for problems P 1,0 0 , P 2,0 0 , P 2,1 0 , P 2,2 0 and reciprocally: *Theorems [3.3.1,](#page-36-0) [3.3.2,](#page-37-0) [3.3.5,](#page-39-0) [3.3.6,](#page-39-1) [3.3.9,](#page-40-0) [3.3.10,](#page-40-1) [3.3.11,](#page-41-0) [3.3.12](#page-41-1)*;

- 8 Theorems for conditions such that efficient solution of problem P 0,0 0 remains efficient for problems P 1,0 1 , P 2,0 1 , P 2,1 1 , P 2,2 1 and reciprocally: *Theorems [3.4.3,](#page-43-0) [3.4.4,](#page-44-0) [3.4.5,](#page-46-0) [3.4.6,](#page-46-1) [3.4.7,](#page-47-0) [3.4.8,](#page-47-1) [3.4.9,](#page-48-0) [3.4.10](#page-48-1)*;
- 8 Theorems for conditions such that efficient solution of problem P 0,0 0 remain efficient for problems P 1,0 2 , P 2,0 2 , P 2,1 2 , P 2,2 2 and reciprocally: *Theorems [3.5.3,](#page-52-0) [3.5.4,](#page-54-0) [3.5.5,](#page-55-0) [3.5.6,](#page-56-0) [3.5.7,](#page-56-1) [3.5.8,](#page-57-0) [3.5.9,](#page-57-1) [3.5.10](#page-58-0)*;

8 Theorems to study relations between different order approximate problems: *Theorems [3.6.1,](#page-61-0) [3.6.2,](#page-62-0) [3.6.3,](#page-63-0) [3.6.4,](#page-64-0) [3.6.5,](#page-64-1) [3.6.6,](#page-65-0) [3.6.7,](#page-66-0) [3.6.8](#page-67-0)*;

• 7 counter-examples to emphasize the importance of "invexitytype conditions": *Examples [3.3.3,](#page-38-0) [3.3.7,](#page-39-2) [3.4.11,](#page-49-0) [3.4.12,](#page-50-0) [3.5.11,](#page-59-0) [3.5.12,](#page-59-1) [3.5.13](#page-60-0)*.

## <span id="page-70-0"></span>**Chapter 4**

# **Minimax model for energy optimization**

## <span id="page-70-1"></span>**4.1 Introduction**

This chapter is presenting minimax, our first energy optimization model. It is a bi-criteria problem, which aims to shave the peak load by minimizing fluctuation of energy and maximizing economic performance of power plant. Its efficient solution represents the amount of energy to be produced at each time moment, such that fluctuation is minimized and turnover maximized. Additionally, this solution owns the capacity to shave the peak load and thus the objective of our research will be fulfilled.

Targeting the peak load and shaving it require a proper measure for fluctuation of energy. Previous chapter shows that maximum absolute deviation might be a starting point to develop that measure.

Economic literature and practice are considering profit, turnover, cost or rate of return as possible measures for economic performance of a company. When choosing the appropriate measure, we have to consider which data will be required to perform tests for validating minimax. Of course profit would provide the most accurate solution, but access to specific costs of production and storage is impossible for us at this moment. Therefor we will use turnover, being aware that accuracy of solution will suffer.

A simple technical constraint, which limits the amount of energy to be produced at each time moment, will be employed. More complex technical constraints require specific knowledge and competences in energy field.

To solve the minimax model, we will transform it in an equivalent parametric problem, which will be solved using Kuhn-Tucker conditions. Convexity of parametric problem is ensuring that Kuhn-Tucker conditions are both necessary and sufficient.

Tests on real data will be performed to evaluate performances of minimax and to validate it.

## <span id="page-71-0"></span>**4.2 Minimax measure for fluctuation of energy**

In our attempt to shave the peak load, we will try to push it down. Thus we will need a measure for fluctuation able to target the peak load and when fluctuation will be minimized the magnitude of peak load will decrease, generating the desired shaving.

From the three measures evaluated in Chapter 2, only maximum absolute deviation proved to be suitable for addressing the peak load.

Energy is a special commodity, with a price very sensitive to quantity, especially around peak load range, as it was exemplified during the heat wave from 2011 in Texas [\[132\]](#page-183-3). Is this hypothesis regarding price confirmed also by other studies? According to [\[152\]](#page-185-10) own-price elasticity for energy has short-run values of −0.12 (year 1), −0.21 (year 2) and −0.24 (year 3) and long- run value of −0.4 (year 25). The meaning of them is that a price increase of 10% will generate for example a 1.2% decrease in consumption over the next year. Deryugina et all [\[35\]](#page-174-9) have presented, during a conference hosted by Energy Institute at Haas Berkeley, that some evidence were found to sustain a symmetric demand response for price increase and price decrease. This analysis is providing enough arguments, for short and long time horizon, to include price of energy in the measure for fluctuation of energy. It will balance between low consumption hours and high consumption hours and will support the optimization process. Measuring unit for fluctuation of energy will be money. It has a connection with practice, because when a customer is modifying an order, a manager is calculating the impact in money and not in quantity (pieces).

Let's consider a predefined level of energy around which we aim to minimize fluctuation of energy.

**Definition 4.2.1** *Minimax fluctuation of energy is the maximum, over all time periods, for difference between energy produced at certain time moments and a predefined level of energy, multiplied with price of energy at the corresponding time moment.*

**Remark 4.2.2** *Predefined level of energy might be for example a random value chosen by energy plant or the average amount of energy produced during a certain period from the past. Of course, when the average is employed, it might be necessary to adjust it with a factor to cover the projected increase of demand.*

Denoting by 1, 2, ..., i, ...., n the time horizon considered and

x<sup>i</sup> - energy produced at time moment i, i = 1, n,

p<sup>i</sup> - price of energy at time moment i, i = 1, n,

r - predefined level of energy,

ε - minimum level of energy assumed by the power plant to be delivered in the power grid,

ρ - maximum level of energy assumed by the power plant to be delivered in the power grid,

<span id="page-72-0"></span>and considering that ≤ r ≤ ρ we have the following mathematical expression for *minimax fluctuation of energy*

$$\max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \left| p\_i x\_i - p\_i r \right|. \tag{4.1}$$

## <span id="page-72-1"></span>**4.3 Problem formulation**

An energy plant focuses the problem of determining the amount of energy to be produced at certain time moments, such that peak load will be shaved by minimizing fluctuation of energy and maximizing economic performance.

It is a bi-criteria problem, where fluctuation has to be minimized and economic performance maximized.

As measure for fluctuation of energy we will use minimax measure ([4.1](#page-72-0)), while as measure for economic performance we will use turnover. Reason for choosing turnover was explained in Section 3.1 (Introduction).

We will use technical constraints which refer to boundaries of energy produced at certain time moments. More advanced technical constraints require specific knowledge and competences in energy field.

Thus we have:

*turnover*

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i$$

*constraints*

<span id="page-73-0"></span>
$$\varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \ i = 1, n$$

and using minimax measure for fluctuation of energy ([4.1](#page-72-0)) we obtain the following mathematical model for our problem

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} |p\_i x\_i - p\_i r| \right), \ -\sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i \right)^T \\\ \varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \ \ i = \overline{1,n}. \end{cases} \tag{4.2}$$

and define it *minimax energy model*.

## <span id="page-73-1"></span>**4.4 Computing the solution**

We recall that for a problem

$$\begin{cases} \min f(x) \\ \quad x \in X \end{cases}$$

where X ⊆ R <sup>n</sup> and f = (f1, f2, ...fm) T : X → R <sup>m</sup>, a feasible solution x <sup>∗</sup> ∈ X is said to be efficient solution if @x ∈ X such that

$$f\left(x\right) \le f\left(x^\*\right)$$

$$f\left(x\right) \ne f\left(x^\*\right).$$

<span id="page-74-1"></span>In order to determine the efficient solution for problem ([4.2](#page-73-0)) we will introduce the following bi-criteria equivalent problem

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( y, \, -\sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i \right)^T \\ \left| p\_i x\_i - p\_i r \right| \le y, \, \, i = \overline{1, n} \\ \varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \, \, i = \overline{1, n}. \end{cases} \tag{4.3}$$

Equivalence between problems ([4.2](#page-73-0)) and ([4.3](#page-74-1)) is shown in the following Lemma.

<span id="page-74-0"></span>**Lemma 4.4.1** *[\[98\]](#page-180-0) Let's consider the bi-criteria optimization problems [\(4.2\)](#page-73-0) and [\(4.3\)](#page-74-1).*

- *a) If* x ∈ R n *is an efficient solution for problem* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*)*, then* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> ×R*, with* y = max i=1,n |pix<sup>i</sup> − pir| *is an efficient solution for problem* (*[4.3](#page-74-1)*)*.*
- *b) If* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R*, with* y = max i=1,n |pix<sup>i</sup> − pir| *is an efficient solution for problem* (*[4.3](#page-74-1)*)*, then* x ∈ R n *is an efficient solution for problem* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*)*.*

### **Proof.**

a) Let x ∈ R <sup>n</sup> be an efficient solution for problem ([4.2](#page-73-0)).

Then @x <sup>0</sup> ∈ R n , with ε ≤ x 0 <sup>i</sup> ≤ ρ, i = 1, n such that

$$\begin{aligned} \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} &|p\_i x\_i^0 - p\_i r| &\le \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} |p\_i x\_i - p\_i r|\\ &\sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i^0 &\ge \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i, \ i = \overline{1,n} \end{aligned}$$

and at least one inequality holds strictly.

Suppose (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R, with y = max i=1,n |pix<sup>i</sup> − pir| is not an efficient solution for problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)).

Then there exists (x 0 , y<sup>0</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R a feasible solution for problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)), with

$$\begin{array}{rcl} y^0 &\le & y\\ \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i^0 &\ge & \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i, \; i = \overline{1,n} \end{array}$$

and at least one inequality holds strictly.

Because (x 0 , y<sup>0</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup>×R is a feasible solution for problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)), it follows that

$$\left| p\_i x\_i^0 - p\_i r \right| \le y^0, \ i = \overline{1, n}$$

which means that

$$\max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \left| p\_i x\_i^0 - p\_i r \right| \le y^0.$$

Thus we obtain that

$$\begin{aligned} \max\_{i=1,n} & \left| p\_i x\_i^0 - p\_i r \right| \le \|y^0 \le y = \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} & \left| p\_i x\_i - p\_i r \right| \\ \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i^0 &\ge \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i, \; i = \overline{1,n} \end{aligned}$$

and at least one inequality holds strictly.

This way we obtain a contradiction for the efficiency of x ∈ R n as solution for ([4.2](#page-73-0)).

In conclusion, (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R, with y = max i=1,n |pix<sup>i</sup> − pir| is an efficient solution for problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)).

b) Let (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R, with y = max i=1,n |pix<sup>i</sup> − pir| be an efficient solution for problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)).

Suppose x ∈ R n is not an efficient solution for problem ([4.2](#page-73-0)) and let x <sup>0</sup> be a feasible solution, with

$$\begin{aligned} \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} & \left| p\_i x\_i^0 - p\_i r \right| \le \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \left| p\_i x\_i - p\_i r \right|, \ i = \overline{1,n}, \\ & \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i^0 \ge \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i, \ i = \overline{1,n} \end{aligned}$$

and at least one inequality holds strictly.

Denoting y <sup>0</sup> = max i=1,n |pix 0 <sup>i</sup> − pir| , it follows

$$\begin{array}{rcl} y^0 &\le & y\\ \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i^0 &\ge & \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i, \; i = \overline{1,n} \end{array}$$

and at least one inequality holds strictly, which contradicts the efficiency of (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R.

In conclusion x ∈ R n is an efficient solution for problem ([4.2](#page-73-0)) and this ends our proof.

<span id="page-76-1"></span>Using Theorem [2.3.1](#page-23-2) of Yu [\[160\]](#page-186-3) and similar results of Bot et all [\[14\]](#page-172-10) and Geoffrion [\[53\]](#page-176-10) the bi-criteria problem ([4.3](#page-74-1)) is equivalent to the following parametric optimization problem

$$\begin{cases} \min \left\{ \lambda y - (1 - \lambda) \sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i \right\} \\ \quad |p\_i x\_i - p\_i r| \le y, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\ \quad \varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, n}. \end{cases} \tag{4.4}$$

<span id="page-76-3"></span>with λ ∈ (0, 1) and the following Lemma holds

**Lemma 4.4.2** *[\[98\]](#page-180-0)* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R *is an efficient solution for bi-criteria problem* (*[4.3](#page-74-1)*) *if and only if* ∃λ ∈ (0, 1) *such that* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R *is an optimal solution for parametric optimization problem* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*)

The meaning of λ in this context is the sensitivity of energy plant for reducing the fluctuation. The bigger λ is, the energy plant is more interested in reducing the fluctuation. The smaller λ is, the energy plant is less interested in reducing the fluctuation and more interested in increasing the turnover.

<span id="page-76-2"></span>**Remark 4.4.3** *Considering the equivalence between problems* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*) *and* (*[4.3](#page-74-1)*)*, respectively problems* (*[4.3](#page-74-1)*) *and* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*)*, it follows from transitivity that problems* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*) *and* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*) *are equivalent. This means that in order to compute the efficient solution for* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*) *we have to determine the optimal solution for* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*)*.*

**Remark 4.4.4** *In the process of computing the optimal solution, we will split the set* {1, 2, ..., n} *in subsets like* {1, 2, ..., l} *and* {l + 1, l + 2, ..., n}*, or* {1, 2, ..., l}*,* {l + 1, l + 2, ..., m} *and* {m + 1, m + 2, ..., n}*. If price is constant on such a set or subset, it will be denoted by* p*.*

<span id="page-76-0"></span>**Theorem 4.4.5 (Luca and Mahalov [\[98\]](#page-180-0);** *parametric minimax***)** *. The optimal solutions for parametric optimization problem* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*) *are:*

*1. If* λ < <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 *, then* ( x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ρ, i = 1, n y <sup>∗</sup> = p (ρ − r)

*or*

$$\bullet \text{ } \sharp \overline{p\_1} \le p\_j, \ j = \overline{l+1, n}, \text{ then}$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left(\rho - r\right) \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l.

• *else problem has no solution.*

$$2. \text{ If } \lambda = \frac{n}{n+1}, then$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1, n}} \left\{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \right\}. \end{cases}$$

\*\*3.\*\*  $\text{If } \lambda > \frac{n}{n+1}$ , then 
$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = 0. \end{cases}$$

$$4. \text{ } If \, \lambda < \frac{l}{l+1}, then$$

$$\bullet \text{ } \sharp p\_j < \overline{p}, \ j = \overline{l+1, n}, \text{ then}$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left( \rho - r \right) \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l.

• *else problem has no solution.*

$$5. \text{ } If \,\lambda = \frac{l}{l+1}, then$$

\* if  $p\_j < p\_i, i = 1, l, \ i = l+1, n$ , then 
$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = \min\_{i=1, l} \{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \}, & if \ p\_j < p\_i \end{cases}$$

• *else problem has no solution*

$$6. \text{ } If \, \lambda < \frac{l + (n - m)}{l + (n - m) + 1}, then$$

• *if* p<sup>j</sup> ≤ p ≤ p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m*, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\ x\_k^\* = \rho, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left(\rho - r\right) \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n.

• *else problem has no solution.*

The proof of this theorem is a 4 steps process.

At first step, for a fixed i from {1, 2, ..., n} all combinations for Kuhn-Tucker multipliers will be identified and their behavior related to complementarity slackness and dual feasibility conditions (([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1))) will be analyzed. The end result for this step will be the *possible combinations.*

<span id="page-78-0"></span>**Definition 4.4.6** Possible combinations *are the combinations of Kuhn-Tucker multipliers, determined for a fixed* i ∈ {1, 2, ..., n} *for which complementarity slackness and dual feasibility conditions are fulfilled.*

At step two, for i = 1, n, the behavior of possible combinations related to the gradient of Lagrangian (([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3))) is analyzed. The end result are the *feasible combinations.*

<span id="page-78-1"></span>**Definition 4.4.7** Feasible combinations *are those possible combinations for which the gradient of Lagrangian is zero.*

<span id="page-78-2"></span>At step three, for i = 1, n, the combining capacity of feasible combinations is analyzed. The end result are the *critical combinations.*

.

**Definition 4.4.8** Critical combinations *are those feasible combinations for which a solution does not exist if they are combined.*

At step four, the optimal solutions are computed based on feasible and critical combinations.

**Proof.** Let λ ∈ (0, 1) fixed. Problem ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is equivalent with the following

$$\begin{cases} \min \left\{ \lambda y - (1 - \lambda) \sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i \right\} \\ \quad - y \le p\_i x\_i - p\_i r, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ p\_i x\_i - p\_i r \le y, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ \varepsilon \le x\_i, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ x\_i \le \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \end{cases}$$

which is a convex optimization problem with inequality constraints.

The associated Kuhn-Tucker conditions are

<span id="page-79-3"></span>
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial x\_i} = -\left(1 - \lambda\right)p\_i - a\_i p\_i + b\_i p\_i - c\_i + d\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{\text{KT1}}$$

<span id="page-79-2"></span>
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial y} = \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^{n} a\_i - \sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i = 0 \tag{KT2}$$

<span id="page-79-0"></span>
$$\left(-y^\* - p\_i x\_i^\* + p\_i r\right) a\_i = 0, \quad a\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{\text{KT3}}$$

$$\left(p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r - y^\*\right) b\_i = 0, \quad b\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{KT4}$$

$$\left(\varepsilon - x\_i^\*\right)c\_i = 0, \quad c\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{KT5}$$

$$\left(x\_i^\* - \rho\right)d\_i = 0, \quad d\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{KT6}$$

<span id="page-79-1"></span>where

$$\begin{array}{rcl} L &:& \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R} \times \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n \longrightarrow \mathbb{R} \\ L \left( x, y, a, b, c, d \right) &=& \lambda y - (1 - \lambda) \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i + \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i \left( -y - p\_i x\_i + p\_i r \right) + \\ & & + \sum\_{i=1}^n b\_i \left( p\_i x\_i - p\_i r - y \right) + \\ & & + \sum\_{i=1}^n c\_i \left( \varepsilon - x\_i \right) + \sum\_{i=1}^n d\_i \left( x\_i - \rho \right) \end{array}$$

is the associated Lagrangian and (x ∗ , y<sup>∗</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R is the optimal solution.

**Remark 4.4.9** *Due to the fact that the optimization problem is a convex one, it follows that Kuhn-Tucker conditions are both necessary and sufficient.*

We have to compute (x ∗ , y<sup>∗</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R in order to determine the optimal solution for our parametric problem ([4.4](#page-76-1)).

*Step 1*

<span id="page-80-0"></span>Let i ∈ {1, 2, ...n} fixed. Then, the possible scenarios for Kuhn-Tucker multipliers are presented in Tabel [4](#page-80-0).1

TABLE 4.1: Combination of KT multipliers for minimax model

We will analyze the behavior of each scenario related to complementarity slackness and dual feasibility conditions (Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1))) and we will determine the solution for each scenario if it exists.

**Scenario 1**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

<span id="page-81-0"></span>
$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases} \tag{4.5}$$

From the first two inequalities of the system we have

$$\begin{aligned} x\_i^\* &\geq \quad r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \\ x\_i^\* &\leq \quad r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \end{aligned}$$

and considering the last two inequalities of the system it follows that

$$y^\* \le \quad p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right)$$

$$y^\* \le \quad p\_i \left(\rho - r\right).$$

Thus, the solution for system ([4.5](#page-81-0)) is

$$\begin{cases} \begin{aligned} &x\_i^\* \in \left[r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}\right], \\ &y^\* \le p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right) \\ &y^\* \le p\_i \left(\rho - r\right). \end{aligned} \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 2**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From the first equation of the system we have

$$x\_i^\* = r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}$$

and replacing it in the other 3 inequations it follows

$$\begin{cases} \begin{array}{c} p\_i \left( r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \right) - p\_i r \leq y^\* \\\ r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \geq \varepsilon \\\ r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \leq \rho \end{array} \end{cases}$$

which is equivalent to

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( r - \varepsilon \right) \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( r - \rho \right) . \end{cases}$$

Because first and third relations are obvious, the following solution is obtained

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r - \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( r - \varepsilon \right) .\end{cases}$$

**Scenario 3**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From the second equation of the system we have

$$x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}$$

and replacing it in the other 3 inequations it follows

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i \left( r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \right) - p\_i r \\\ r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \ge \varepsilon \\\ r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \le \rho \end{cases}$$

which is equivalent to

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right) \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left(\rho - r\right) .\end{cases}$$

Because first two relations are obvious, the following solution is obtained

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i} \\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( \rho - r \right). \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 4**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon$$

it's obvious that 4th inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first two it follows

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i(\varepsilon - r) \\\ p\_i(\varepsilon - r) \le y^\* \end{cases}$$

which is equivalent to

$$\begin{cases} \ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( r - \varepsilon \right) \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( \varepsilon - r \right) . \end{cases}$$

Because the last relation is obvious, the solution for the system is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( r - \varepsilon \right) . \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 5**

$$\begin{array}{|c|c|c|c|c|} \hline a\_i = 0 & b\_i = 0 & c\_i = 0 & d\_i > 0 \\ \hline \end{array}$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

$$x\_i^\* = \rho$$

it's obvious that 3rd inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first two it follows

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i(\rho - r) \\\ p\_i \left(\rho - r\right) \le y^\* \end{cases}$$

which is equivalent to

$$\begin{cases} \ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( r - \rho \right) \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) . \end{cases}$$

Because the first relation is obvious, the solution for the system is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i \left(\rho - r\right). \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 6**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

Adding the first two equations of the system we obtain that

$$x\_i^\* = r$$

and

$$y^\* = 0.$$

It's obvious that x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = r verifies the last two inequalities of the system and thus the solution is

$$\begin{cases} \, \, x\_i^\* = r \\ \, \, y^\* = 0. \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 7**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon$$

it's obvious that 4th inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first equality it follows

$$-y^\* = p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right)$$

which is equivalent to

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right)$$

Replacing in second inequality of the system it follows

$$p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right) \le y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right)$$

which is obvious and therefore the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ y^\* = p\_i \left( r - \varepsilon \right) . \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 8**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

$$x\_i^\* = \rho$$

it's obvious that 3rd inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first equality of the system it follows

$$-y^\* = p\_i \left(\rho - r\right)$$

which is equivalent to

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \rho\right)$$

From second inequality of the system we obtain that

$$p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \le y^\* = p\_i \left( r - \rho \right)^\*$$

which is a contradiction and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 9**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ε

it's obvious that 4th inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the second equality of the system it follows

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right)$$

which is equivalent to

$$-y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right)$$

From first inequality of the system we obtain that

$$p\_i\left(r-\varepsilon\right) = -y^\* \le p\_i\left(\varepsilon - r\right)$$

which is a contradiction and thus system has no solution.

**Scenario 10**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ρ

it's obvious that 3rd inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the second equality of the system it follows

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(\rho - r\right)$$

which is equivalent to

$$-y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \rho\right)$$

From first inequality of the system we obtain that

$$p\_i\left(r-\rho\right) = -y^\* \le p\_i\left(\rho - r\right)$$

which is obvious and thus the solution for the system is

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* = \rho \\ \, \boldsymbol{y}^\* = p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) . \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 11**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> > 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

From 3rd and 4th equations it follows that

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 12**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ε

it's obvious that 4th inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first equality of the system it follows

$$-y^\* = p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right)$$

which is equivalent to

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \varepsilon\right)$$

From second equality of the system we obtain that

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(\varepsilon - r\right)$$

which is a contradiction and thus system has no solution.

**Scenario 13** a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> > 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* \ge \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

Considering

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ρ

it's obvious that 3rd inequality of the system is satisfied. Replacing in the first equality of the system it follows

$$-y^\* = p\_i(\rho - r)$$

which is equivalent to

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(r - \rho\right)$$

From second equality of the system we obtain that

$$y^\* = p\_i \left(\rho - r\right)$$

which is a contradiction and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 14**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* \le p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

From 3rd and 4th equations it follows that

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 15**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> > 0 The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \le y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

From 3rd and 4th equations it follows that

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 16**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT3](#page-79-0) ) to ([KT6](#page-79-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -y^\* = p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r \\ p\_i x\_i^\* - p\_i r = y^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ x\_i^\* = \rho \end{cases}$$

From 3rd and 4th equations it follows that

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

From the 16 scenarios, we have proved that only 8 are *possible combinations*. These are 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7 and 10.

*Step 2*

For i = 1, n we will analyze the behavior of possible combinations related to the gradient of Lagrangian (Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3))).

### **Scenario 1**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\ \lambda = 0 \end{cases}$$

and thus λ = 0 and p<sup>i</sup> = 0, i = 1, n, which is impossible.

**Scenario 2**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1 - \lambda\right)p\_i - a\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i = 0 \end{cases}$$

and thus a<sup>i</sup> = − (1 − λ) < 0, which is impossible.

**Scenario 3**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1 - \lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^n b\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

Then <sup>b</sup><sup>i</sup> = (1 <sup>−</sup> <sup>λ</sup>), i <sup>=</sup> <sup>1</sup>, n, and thus <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> i=1 b<sup>i</sup> = n (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda = \frac{n}{n+1}.
$$

**Scenario 4**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i - c\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\\ \lambda = 0. \end{cases}$$

and thus c<sup>i</sup> = − (1 − λ) p<sup>i</sup> < 0, i = 1, n, which is impossible.

**Scenario 5**

$$\begin{array}{c|c|c|c} \hline a\_i = 0 & b\_i = 0 & c\_i = 0 & d\_i > 0 \\ \hline \end{array}$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + d\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\ \lambda = 0. \end{cases}$$

and thus d<sup>i</sup> = (1 − λ) p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n. But λ = 0 and the system has no solution.

### **Scenario 6**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 d<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i - a\_i p\_i + b\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i - \sum\_{i=1}^n b\_i = 0 \end{cases}$$

and thus

$$a\_i = b\_i - \left(1 - \lambda\right), \ i = \overline{1, n}.$$

It follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{n} a\_i = \sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i - n\left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i + n\left(1 - \lambda\right) - \sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i = 0
$$

which is equivalent to

$$
\lambda + n\left(1 - \lambda\right) - 2\sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i = 0.
$$

But a<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, n and then b<sup>i</sup> > (1 − λ), i = 1, n, which means that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i > n \left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Thus we have

$$
\lambda + n\left(1 - \lambda\right) - 2n\left(1 - \lambda\right) > 0
$$

equivalent to

$$
\lambda - n \left( 1 - \lambda \right) > 0.
$$

In conclusion

$$
\lambda > \frac{n}{n+1}.
$$

**Scenario 7**

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3))

is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i - a\_i p\_i - c\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i = 0 \end{cases}$$

and thus c<sup>i</sup> = −p<sup>i</sup> [a<sup>i</sup> + (1 − λ)] < 0, i = 1, n, which is impossible.

**Scenario 10**

$$
\begin{array}{|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
a\_i = 0 & b\_i > 0 & c\_i = 0 & d\_i > 0 \\
\hline
\cdot & \cdot & \bullet & \bullet & \bullet & \bullet \\
\hline
\end{array}
$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i + d\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n}, \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^n b\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

Then

$$d\_i = p\_i \left[ (1 - \lambda) - b\_i \right], \ i = \overline{1, n}$$

and because d<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, n it follows

$$b\_i < (1 - \lambda)$$

and thus

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{n} b\_i < n\left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

From the last equation of the system we obtain

$$
\lambda < \frac{n}{n+1}.
$$

<span id="page-93-0"></span>Synthesizing the behavior of the 8 possible combinations, related to Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)), the situation presented in Tabel [4](#page-93-0).2 is obtained

TABLE 4.2: Behavior of possible combinations related to gradient of Lagrangian for minimax model

**Remark 4.4.10** *For scenario 5 we notice that* (*[KT2](#page-79-3)*) *is not satisfied, which means that scenario 5 will not generate a solution by its own, but combined with other scenarios, it might generate a solution.*

Thus, the feasible scenarios which will generate the optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) are 3, 5, 6 and 10.

*Step 3*

We state that the critical combinations are 6 with 5 and 6 with 10. We will prove only one, the other being similar.

For i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n combination 6 with 5 is composed from

scenario 6 which has the solution

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ y^\* = 0 \end{cases}$$

and scenario 5 which has the solution

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

It follows that the conditions to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{cases} \ y^\* = 0\\ \ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

which means that

$$p\_j\left(\rho - r\right) \le 0, \ j = \overline{l+1, n}$$

but this is impossible.

*Step 4*

Before computing optimal solutions based on feasible combinations and critical combinations we will prove that the order of scenarios in a combination does not change the solution. We will work with combination 10 with 3, the others being similar.

For combination 10 with 3, as we will notice in the following part of the demonstration for our Theorem, the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left(\rho - r\right), \; if \; \overline{p\_1} \le p\_j \end{cases}$$

where p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l.

What we notice here is that the small prices are constant and on that interval the energy is produced at maximum level to be delivered in the power grid. On the other price interval, energy is computed based on x ∗ <sup>j</sup> = r + y ∗ pj , j = l + 1, n.

For combination 3 with 10 we will analyze in detail the computation of solution. For 3, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), & i = \overline{1, l} \end{cases}$$

and for 10 with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = p\_j \left(\rho - r\right), & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$
