Because y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> (ρ − r), j = l + 1, n we state that all prices are constant on the set {l + 1, l + 2, ...n} and denote p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n. In order to have all conditions for y ∗ fulfilled it is necessary that

$$
\overline{p\_2} \le p\_i, \ i = \overline{1, l}.
$$

Chosing

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_2} \left( \rho - r \right)$$

a solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_2} \left(\rho - r\right), & if \, \overline{p\_2} \le p\_i. \end{cases}$$

We notice again that the small prices are constant and on that interval the energy is produced at maximum level to be delivered in the power grid. On the other price interval, energy is computed based on x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = r + y ∗ pi , i = 1, l.

In conclusion we have proved that order of combination does not influence the solution, result which will be formulated as a Theorem and consequently used and addressed.

The combinations based on which we will compute the optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)), are: 3, 6, 10, 6+3, 10+3, 10+5, 3+5 and 3+5+10.

For 3, denoting by

$$y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \{ p\_i(\rho - r) \},$$

an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1, n}} \left\{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \right\} \end{cases}$$

if λ = n <sup>n</sup>+1 .

For 6, an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = 0 \end{cases}$$

if λ > <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 .

For 10, from y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> (ρ − r), i = 1, n we state that all prices are constant and denoting by p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n, the optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left( \rho - r \right) \end{cases}$$

if λ < <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 .

For combination 6 with 3, if i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i - a\_i p\_i + b\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + b\_j p\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \sum\_{i=1}^l b\_i - \sum\_{j=l+1}^n b\_j = 0. \end{cases}$$

Thus

$$a\_i = b\_i - \left(1 - \lambda\right), \ i = \overline{1, l}$$

and

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} a\_i = \sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i - l\left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Because a<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, l it follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i > l \left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Also

$$b\_j = \left(1 - \lambda\right), \ j = \overline{l+1, n}$$

and then

$$\sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} b\_j = \left(n-l\right)\left(1-\lambda\right).$$

Using now the last equation of the system it follows

$$
\lambda > \frac{n}{n+1}.
$$

For 6, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ y^\* = 0 \end{cases}$$

and for 3, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \le p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), & j = \overline{l+1, n}. \end{cases}$$

Chosing y <sup>∗</sup> = 0 it follows that

$$x\_j^\* = r, \; j = \overline{l+1, n}$$

and

$$0 \le p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \ j = \overline{l+1, n}$$

which is obvious.

Then, an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = 0. \end{cases}$$

if λ > <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 .

For combination 10 with 3, if i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i + d\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + b\_j p\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^l b\_i - \sum\_{j=l+1}^n b\_j = 0. \end{cases}$$

Thus

$$d\_i = p\_i \left[ (1 - \lambda) - b\_i \right], \ i = 1, l$$

and because d<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, l it follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i < l \left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Also

$$b\_j = \left(1 - \lambda\right), \; j = \overline{l+1, n}$$

and then

$$\sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} b\_j = (n-l)\left(1-\lambda\right).$$

Using now the last equation of the system it follows

$$
\lambda < \frac{n}{n+1}.
$$

For 10, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* = p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), & i = \overline{1, l} \end{cases}$$

and for 3, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \le p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

Because y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> (ρ − r), i = 1, l we state that all prices are constant on the set {1, 2, ...l} and denote p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l. In order to have all conditions for y ∗ fulfilled it is necessary that

$$
\overline{p\_1} \le p\_j, \ j = \overline{l+1, n}.
$$

Chosing

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left( \rho - r \right)$$

an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left(\rho - r\right), \; if \, \overline{p\_1} \le p\_j, \end{cases}$$

if λ < <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 .

For combination 10 with 5, if i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i + d\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + d\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

Thus

$$d\_i = p\_i \left[ (1 - \lambda) - b\_i \right], \ i = \overline{1, l}$$

and because d<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, l it follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i < l \left(1 - \lambda\right).$$

Using now the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda < \frac{l}{l+1}.
$$

For 10, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} \; x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\\; y^\* = p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), \quad i = \overline{1,l} \end{cases}$$

and for 5, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

Because y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> (ρ − r), i = 1, l we state that all prices are constant on the set {1, 2, ...l} and denote p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l. In order to have all conditions for y ∗ fulfilled it is necessary that

$$p\_j \le \overline{p\_1}, \; j = \overline{l+1, n}.$$

Chosing

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left( \rho - r \right)$$

an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left( \rho - r \right), \; if \; p\_j < \overline{p\_1} \end{cases}$$

if λ < <sup>l</sup> <sup>l</sup>+1 .

For combination 3 with 5, if i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + d\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^l b\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

Thus

$$b\_i = \left(1 - \lambda\right), \ i = \overline{1, l}$$

and it follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i = l \left( 1 - \lambda \right).$$

Using now the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda = \frac{l}{l+1}.
$$

For 3, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} \begin{array}{c} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), \quad i = \overline{1, l} \end{array} \end{cases}$$

and for 5, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} .\end{cases}$$

The conditions to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{aligned} y^\* \le p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), \quad j = \overline{l+1, n}. \end{aligned}$$

If p<sup>i</sup> ≤ p<sup>j</sup> , i = 1, l, j = l + 1, n, then @y ∗ . If p<sup>i</sup> > p<sup>j</sup> , i = 1, l, j = l + 1, n, then by chosing

$$y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} \{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \}$$

an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} \left\{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \right\}, & if \ p\_j < p\_i, \end{cases}$$

if λ = l <sup>l</sup>+1 .

For combination 3 with 5 and with 10, if i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m and k = m + 1, n, then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([KT1](#page-79-2)) and ([KT2](#page-79-3)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + b\_i p\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + d\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_k + b\_k p\_k + d\_k = 0, \quad k = \overline{m+1,n} \\ \lambda - \sum\_{i=1}^l b\_i - \sum\_{k=m+1}^n b\_k = 0. \end{cases}$$

Thus

$$b\_i = \left(1 - \lambda\right), \ i = \overline{1, l}$$

and it follows that

$$\sum\_{i=1}^{l} b\_i = l \left( 1 - \lambda \right).$$

Also

$$d\_k = p\_k \left[ (1 - \lambda) - b\_k \right], \ k = \overline{m + 1, n}$$

and because d<sup>k</sup> > 0, k = m + 1, n it follows that

$$\sum\_{k=m+1}^{n} b\_k < \left(n-m\right)\left(1-\lambda\right).$$

Using now the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda < \frac{l + (n - m)}{l + (n - m) + 1}.
$$

For 3, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \le p\_i \left( \rho - r \right), & i = \overline{1, l} \end{cases}$$

for 5, with j = l + 1, m the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j \left( \rho - r \right), & j = \overline{l+1, m} \end{cases}$$

and for 10, with k = m + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_k^\* = \rho, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = p\_k \left(\rho - r\right), & k = \overline{m+1, n} \end{cases}$$

Because y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>k</sup> (ρ − r), k = m + 1, n we state that all prices are constant on the set {m + 1, m + 2, ...n} and denote p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n. In order to have all conditions for y ∗ fulfilled it is necessary that

$$\begin{array}{rcl}\overline{p\_3} & \le & p\_i, \ i = \overline{1, l} \\\ p\_j & \le & \overline{p\_3}, \ j = \overline{l+1, m}. \end{array}$$

Chosing

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_3} \left( \rho - r \right)$$

an optimal solution for ([4.4](#page-76-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ x\_k^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_3} \left( \rho - r \right), & if \ p\_j \le \overline{p\_3} \le p\_i \end{cases}$$

,

if λ < <sup>l</sup>+(n−m) <sup>l</sup>+(n−m)+1 .

Thus, the proof of our theorem is complete.

### <span id="page-102-0"></span>**Theorem 4.4.11 (Luca and Mahalov [\[98\]](#page-180-0);** *order of scenarios***)** *.*

*The order of scenarios, used to compute an optimal solution for parametric optimization problem* (*[4.4](#page-76-1)*)*, does not influence the solution.*

**Proof.** The proof of this theorem was made during the proof of Theorem [4.4.5,](#page-76-0) at the beginning of Step 4.

In the following, TR (total revenue) is the notation for turnover. Based on *Remark* [4.4.3](#page-76-2) and *Theorem* [4.4.5](#page-76-0) the following result is true.

### <span id="page-103-0"></span>**Theorem 4.4.12 (Luca and Mahalov [\[98\]](#page-180-0);** *energy minimax***)** *.*

*The efficient solution for bi-criteria energy optimization problem* (*[4.2](#page-73-0)*) *is*

*1. If* λ < <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 *and there is a single price for energy, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, n} \\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left( \rho - r \right) \\ TR = n \overline{p} \rho \end{cases}$$

*or*

• *if energy is sold against two different prices during 24 hours and* p<sup>1</sup> ≤ p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n*, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_j}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1} \left(\rho - r\right) \\\ TR = l\overline{p\_1}\rho + r \sum\_{j=l+1}^n p\_j + \left(n - l\right)y^\* \end{cases}$$

.

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l.

- *else problem has no solution.*
- *2. If* λ = n <sup>n</sup>+1 *, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, n} \\ y^\* = \min\_{i=\overline{1, n}} \{ p\_i \left( \rho - r \right) \} \\ TR = r \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i + n y^\*. \end{cases}$$

*3. If* λ > <sup>n</sup> <sup>n</sup>+1 *, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r, & i = \overline{1, n} \\ y^\* = 0 \\ TR = r \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i. & \end{cases}$$

*4. If* λ < <sup>l</sup> <sup>l</sup>+1 *, then*

$$\bullet \text{ if } p\_j < \overline{p\_1}, \ j = \overline{l+1, n}, \text{ then}$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = \overline{p} \left(\rho - r\right) \\ TR = l\overline{p}\rho + \rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^n p\_j. \end{cases}$$

pj .

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l.

• *else problem has no solution.*

*5. If* λ = l <sup>l</sup>+1 *, then* • *if* p<sup>j</sup> < p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, j = l + 1, n*, then* x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = r + y ∗ pi , i = 1, l x ∗ <sup>j</sup> = ρ, j = l + 1, n y <sup>∗</sup> = min i=1,l {p<sup>i</sup> (ρ − r)} T R = ly<sup>∗</sup> + r P l i=1 p<sup>i</sup> + ρ Pn j=l+1

• *else problem has no solution.*

## 6. If  $\lambda < \frac{l + (n - m)}{l + (n - m) + 1}$ , then 
$$\begin{array}{l} \bullet \text{ if } p\_j \le \overline{p\_3} \le p\_i, \ i = \overline{1, l}, j = \overline{l + 1, m}, \text{ then} \\\\ \begin{cases} x\_i^\* = r + \frac{y^\*}{p\_i}, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l + 1, m} \\ x\_k^\* = \rho, \quad k = \overline{m + 1, n} \\ y^\* = \overline{p}(\rho - r) \\\ T R = l y^\* + r \sum\_{i = 1}^l p\_i + \rho \sum\_{j = l + 1}^n p\_j + (n - m) \overline{p}\rho. \end{array}$$

*where* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n.

• *else problem has no solution.*

**Proof.** The values for (x ∗ , y<sup>∗</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R are computed based on Theorem [4.4.5.](#page-76-0) Values for turnover TR are computed by replacing x <sup>∗</sup> ∈ R n into <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> i=1 pix ∗ i .

## <span id="page-105-0"></span>**4.5 Model validation and conclusions**

### <span id="page-105-1"></span>**4.5.1 Testing of solution**

Due to confidential character of production data specific for power plants, we have to use data corresponding to the entire Romanian energy market. Thus, we will consider the Romanian electricity system as being a single producer. Transelectrica [\[148\]](#page-185-5) is providing real time data for production and consumption of electricity on Romanian market. In our analysis we will use data from the period 10th of November 2007, (time 22:18:00) to 4th of December 2015, (time 23:52:55). Considering the records performed every 10 to 15 minutes, a sample of 415789 values is generated.

The first step in our analysis is to check if data referring to production and consumption contain anomalies (outliers and extreme outliers). A Box & Whisker test is performed to identify them. It is based on computing the average and quartiles and identifying values situated outside the interval

$$(Q\_1 - 1.5 \* IQR; Q\_3 + 1.5 \* IQR)\_{\prime}$$

where IQR = Q<sup>3</sup> − Q1.

Test performed on the sample, using Statgraphics Centurion software, is showing that both outliers and extreme outliers are present in our sample. Figures [4.1](#page-105-2) and [4.2](#page-106-0) are presenting the Box & Whisker diagrams for production respectively consumption, emphasizing the existence of the above mentioned anomalies.

<span id="page-105-2"></span>FIGURE 4.1: Box & Whisker diagram for production of energy

The second step in our analysis is to evaluate if anomalies might be excluded from the sample. Performing a Grubbs test, six outliers

<span id="page-106-0"></span>FIGURE 4.2: Box & Whisker diagram for consumption of energy

and extreme outliers for production, respectively consumption are identified, with a 95% confidence level and eliminated. Due to the fact that they correspond to the same time moment, we might assume that there were some faults in data registration. Thus, for the validated data, the extreme values are 3666 MW and 9551 MW - minimum respectively maximum consumption and 3671 MW and 10808 MW - minimum respectively maximum production.

We can proceed now to the third steep, which represents testing of solutions capacity to shave the peak load. We will need some input data for the algorithm – values for ε, ρ, r and price levels.

The following values for input parameters of *Minimax model* will be used:

- ε = 3666 MW (minimum consumption, based on validated values, over the entire time period)
- ρ = 10808 MW (maximum production, based on validated values, over the entire time period)
- r = 6970 MW (average production, based on validated values, over the entire time period)

For price of electricity the values established by Order 40 issued in 21st of June 2013 by Romanian Energy Regulatory Authority [\[129\]](#page-183-10) will be used. They are presented in Appendix [A.](#page-170-0)

To evaluate the performances, optimal production plan provided by minimax will be compared with real data from 4th of December 2015. For a professional and efficient evaluation, results will be divided in three categories: weak, good and excellent.

**Weak result** means that efficient solution is far from real data. **Good result** means that efficient solution is shaving the peak and flattering the production curve.

**Excellent result** means that efficient solution is shaving the peak and following the production curve as flat as possible.

Theorem [4.4.5](#page-76-0) is providing an optimal production plan. Considering the six solutions provided by the theorem and the possibility to choose different price plans according to conditions required by the theorem, twelve different analysis were performed. A chart was generated for each result for a better visualization. Red color is used to represent the optimal production plan, blue represents the real production curve and green the real consumption curve. They are presented in the following figures and some explanations are presented.

<span id="page-107-0"></span>FIGURE 4.3: Minimax excellent results

Using Price Plan III solutions 1 and 2 from Theorem [4.4.5](#page-76-0) are providing the excellent results. Analyzing both charts from Figure [4.3,](#page-107-0) corresponding to excellent results, it is obvious that our objective to shave the peak load is achieved. Of course fluctuation of energy is reduced and the optimized production plans are maximizing turnover. Additionally, the optimized production curve is following the real production curve as flat as possible. During gap periods, the low price of energy is forcing a boost of production in order to satisfy the maximization of turnover. Shaving the peak load is determining a shift of production and a supra-production of energy at some time moments. According to the strategy of producer, he might choose: to store the energy and to reuse it for covering demand when necessary (ESS), to manually interfere for adjusting the production plan or to initiate a campaign to influence and change the behavior of consumers (DSM).

Let's notice that both excellent results are obtained for Plan III. We will come back to this topic later in our work.

<span id="page-108-0"></span>FIGURE 4.4: Minimax good results

Using Price Plan II, solutions 1 and 2 are providing again results evaluated, this time, with good. Figure [4.4](#page-108-0) is presenting the charts for them. Again it is visible that peak load of production is shaved. Thus we might conclude that our objective is realized.

A deeper analysis reveals interesting aspects. Optimized production curve is not following the real production curve as consistent as previous results. It is due to the reduced variety of tariffs in the pricing plan. This is proving that our decision to include price in the measure of fluctuation is helpful in the optimization process and it was a correct one. The area of inconsistent follow is generating additional over production which has to be addressed according to the strategy of producer.

Corresponding to the night hours, when price is low, over production is present again. It is a similar situation with gap period from Plan III and is generated by the correlation between price, quantity and turnover. Possibilities to address this aspect, presented for the previous results, might be used also in this case.

Optimized production plan is situated in some points below the consumption curve which is very risky. A fine tune of input data, and especially predefined level r, in this case, will improve the solution and mitigate the risk. In this point we can have a first glance to the sensitivity aspects of the model.

Charts from Figure [4.5](#page-109-0) are presenting solutions evaluated with weak results. Optimal production plan is computed either to minimum or maximum levels assumed by the power plant, or to the predefined level. It is obvious that they can not be accepted from practical point of view. Interesting to be mentioned is that solution 1

<span id="page-109-0"></span>FIGURE 4.5: Minimax weak results

and 2, combined with Plan I offers weak results. So again price is a critical key.

Tests performed for minimax model and part of the analysis presented in this subsection were disseminated in [\[90\]](#page-179-1).

Minimax model has generated results evaluated in all three categories, excellent, good and weak. Minimax is realizing the objectiv of our research: to shave the peak load of energy by minimizing fluctuation of energy and maximizing economic performance.

Thus it can be validate as a reliable model for shaving the peak load. Besides planning and economic advantages associated to peak load shaving, as visible in Figures [4.3](#page-107-0) and [4.4,](#page-108-0) we consider that minimax model generates several additional advantages like: (i) incentives for power plants to develop storage systems, (ii) incentives to invest in green energy to compensate the production shift, to reduce CO<sup>2</sup> emissions and to reduce production cost, (iii) reduced fatigue, maintenance and development costs for equipments of power plant (iv) increased efficiency for power plant or (v) a more stable and efficient power grid.

Model is sensitive to input data and price has a key role in optimization. The more price range are available, the better the behavior of solutions is.

### <span id="page-110-0"></span>**4.5.2 Conclusions**

Minimax is a bi-criteria optimization problem which aims to shave the peak load by minimizing fluctuation of energy around a predefined level and maximizing economic performance of power plant.

A proper measure to address the peak load was developed, starting from maximum absolute deviation. Price of energy was introduced in the measure, due to its sensitivity to quantity and its elasticity. Defined by [4](#page-72-0).1 it was called minimax measure for fluctuation of energy.

Economic performance was evaluated using turnover.

As constraints, we have used only a limitation for energy production, between a minimum and a maximum amount assumed by the power plant to be delivered in the power grid.

Being a bi-criteria optimization problem, a Pareto efficient solution was computed.

Using several equivalences, the minimax energy model [\(4](#page-73-0).2) was transformed in the parametric problem [\(4](#page-76-1).4), for which Theorem [4.4.5](#page-76-0) is providing the optimal solution. It computes the amount of energy to be produced at each time moment and the corresponding fluctuation. It is the central theorem of Chapter 4 and its proof is based on

the Kuhn-Tucker conditions, being a 4 steps process. Because parametric problem (4.[4\)](#page-76-1) is a convex one, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions are necessary and sufficient.

At first step we have evaluated, at a fixed moment of time (a fixed value for i = 1, n), the existence of solutions for all Kuhn-Tucker multipliers. The combinations which have generated a solution were called *possible combinations*.

The second step is evaluating, over the entire time period, the behavior of possible Kuhn-Tucker combinations related to the Lagrangian. Those for which the Lagrangian is zero were called *feasible combinations*.

The third step is analyzing the combining capacity of feasible combinations and *critical combinations* are identified.

Forth step is computing the optimal solution for the parametric problem (4.[4\)](#page-76-1), if the same *feasible combination* is multiplied over the entire time period or if different *feasible combinations* are combined during the time horizon.

During the forth step, we had to prove that the order of combining the scenarios does not influence the solution, result presented as Theorem [4.4.11.](#page-102-0)

Returning to the initial minimax energy problem [\(4](#page-73-0).2), Theorem [4.4.12](#page-103-0) is computing the efficient frontier.

Theoretical results obtained in Theorems [4.4.5](#page-76-0) and [4.4.12](#page-103-0) were tested using real data. The confidential character of data related to energy production has determined us to use data corresponding to the entire Romanian market. They were provided by Transelectrica [\[148\]](#page-185-5) and correspond to the period 10th of November 2007, (time 22:18:00) to 4th of December 2015, (time 23:52:55) and by ANRE [\[129\]](#page-183-10). Using Box & Whisker and Grubbs tests, production and consumption data were analyzed and validated for generating inputs in the mimax model. Optimal production plan provided by minimax was compared to real production and consumption curves from 4th of December 2015. According to the their behavior, results were evaluated as excellent, good or weak.

Section 3.4.1 is providing a detailed test and analysis. Minimax model is realizing the objectives of our research, by shaving the peak load, flattering the production and maximizing economic performances. Excellent performances are obtained for price plans with three tariffs

and good performances are obtained for price plans with two tariffs. Shaving the peak load is generating a shift in production with associated supra-production, which can be addressed by methods presented in Chapter 1.

A simple visualization of solutions provided by Theorem [4.4.5](#page-76-0) shows that solutions 3 and 4 will never generate good or excellent results, due to the fact that optimal production plan is placed either to minimum or maximum amount assumed or to the predefined level.

Minimax model has three types of input data, making it more complex to be used in practice, but results provided are confirmed, by tests on real data, to be good and excellent. Model is sensitive to input data, but in the same time, a fine tune of input data, has the capacity to improve even more the behavior of solution.

Adding additional complex technical constraints or using profit as measure for economic performance the complexity of the model will increase, but solutions will be more accurate.

Our contributions to this chapter, presented at International Conference on Approximation Theory and its Applications, organized in Sibiu, Romania during 26-29 May 2016 and disseminated in [\[90\]](#page-179-1) and [\[98\]](#page-180-0), might be synthesized in:

- using of bi-criteria optimization problems in research for shaving the energy peak load,
- introduction of minimax measure of fluctuation [\(4](#page-72-0).1),
- development of minimax energy model (4.[2\)](#page-73-0),
- Lemma [4.4.1,](#page-74-0) which together with other known results of Yu [\[160\]](#page-186-3), Bot [\[14\]](#page-172-10), Geoffrion [\[53\]](#page-176-10) prove the equivalency between minimax energy model [\(4](#page-73-0).2) and parametric model (4.[4\)](#page-76-1),
- Theorem [4.4.5](#page-76-0) which computes the optimal solution for parametric model (4.[4\)](#page-76-1),
- Theorem [4.4.11](#page-102-0) which proves that order of scenarios does not change the solution,
- Theorem [4.4.12](#page-103-0) which computes the efficient solution for minimax energy model (4.[2\)](#page-73-0),
- validation of minimax model after performing tests on real data.

## <span id="page-113-0"></span>**Chapter 5**

# **Index model for energy optimization**

## <span id="page-113-1"></span>**5.1 Introduction**

This Chapter is presenting index model. It is a bi-criteria optimization problem, which aims to shave the peak load by minimizing fluctuation of energy and maximizing economic performance.

Presence of predefined level in minimax measure for fluctuation ([4](#page-72-0).1) increases the complexity of input data for Minimax model ([4](#page-73-0).2), making it difficult to be implemented. Skipping the predefined level will reduce complexity of the model, but in the same time will eliminate the reference point for peak load. We will replace this reference point (the predefined level) with another indicator, more friendly for practitioners.

Skipping the predefined level might increase the range of solution, because maximum absolute deviation, as measure for fluctuation of energy, combined with turnover as measure for economic performance, will never allow a point situated under the predefined level to be Pareto efficient. It is due to absolute value considered in the measure of fluctuation, which makes that a point situated under the predefined level, will always generate a smaller turnover compared with its symmetric, while the fluctuation is the same.

For the same reasons presented in Chapter 4, turnover will measure the economic performance of the power plant and only simple technical constraints, limiting the amount of energy to be produced at certain time moments, will be employed.

Index, our second model for shaving the peak load, is developed in the effort of simplifying minimax and making it more friendly for practitioners.

The idea for the new reference point for peak load and thus for the new measure of energy fluctuation was inspired by production environment, where is common to report loading of an equipment or department to a fixed capacity.

Index will be tested using the same real data as for minimax.

A comparison between minimax and index models, emphasizing the conceptual differences, has been presented by the author during the *12th Edition of ICATA 2016 Conference* and published in [\[39\]](#page-174-0).

## <span id="page-114-1"></span>**5.2 Index measure for fluctuation of energy**

Our aim, is to skip that predefined level around which the fluctuation of energy is minimized and to introduce a new indicator as reference for the peak load. This way complexity of input data will be reduced, range of solution will be extended and a more friendly and easy to be implemented model will be developed.

The optimization process is related to production capacities, for which is quite common to report nominal capacity to a fixed capacity (in general the maximum capacity), as mentioned by Morrison [\[109\]](#page-181-9) and Muchiri [\[112\]](#page-181-10).

<span id="page-114-0"></span>Using the notations introduced in Chapter 4, and the previous mentioned idea, we propose, as a measure for fluctuation the following

$$\max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \left\{ \frac{x\_i}{\rho} p\_i \right\}.\tag{5.1}$$

and call it *index measure for fluctuation of energy*.

Based on sensitivity of price to quantity and elasticity of energy price, explained in the Chapter 4, price is maintained also in the new measure. The measuring unit for index measure of energy fluctuation is money.

## <span id="page-114-2"></span>**5.3 Problem formulation**

To avoid burdens generated by the peak load, a power plant aims to shave it by minimizing fluctuation of energy and maximizing economic performance.

As measure for fluctuation of energy we will use index measure [\(5](#page-114-0).1), while turnover is expressed as

<span id="page-115-0"></span>
$$\sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i.$$

The only constraints imposed for the amount of energy to be produced are

$$
\varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \ i = \overline{1, n}.
$$

Thus, the mathematical model for index energy problem is

$$\begin{cases} \min \left( \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \left\{ \frac{x\_i}{\rho} p\_i \right\}; -\sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i \right)^T\\ \quad \varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \ i = \overline{1,n}. \end{cases} \tag{5.2}$$

## <span id="page-115-2"></span>**5.4 Computing the solution**

In order to determine the efficient solution for the problem [\(5](#page-115-0).2) we introduce the following bi-criteria optimization problem

<span id="page-115-3"></span>
$$\begin{cases} \min \left( y; -\sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i \right)^T \\ \frac{x\_i}{\rho} p\_i \le y, \ i = \overline{1, n} \\ \varepsilon \le x\_i \le \rho, \ i = \overline{1, n} \end{cases} \tag{5.3}$$

The equivalence between (5.[2\)](#page-115-0) and [\(5](#page-115-3).3) is established by the following Lemma.

<span id="page-115-1"></span>**Lemma 5.4.1** *[\[92\]](#page-179-0) Let's consider the bi-criteria optimization problems*[\(5](#page-115-0).2) *and* [\(5](#page-115-3).3)*.*

- *a) If* x ∈ R n *is an efficient solution for problem* (5.[2\)](#page-115-0)*, then* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R*, with* y = max i=1,n n xi ρ pi o *is an efficient solution for problem* [\(5](#page-115-3).3)*.*
- *b) If* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R*, with* y = max i=1,n n xi ρ pi o *is an efficient solution for problem* [\(5](#page-115-3).3)*, then* x ∈ R n *is an efficient solution for problem* [\(5](#page-115-0).2)*.*

**Proof.** Proof is similar with the one of Lemma [4.4.1](#page-74-0) from Chapter 4, so we will not present it in details here.

Using again the idea to transform the bi-criteria problem into a parametric one and based on Theorem [2](#page-23-2).3.1 of Yu [\[160\]](#page-186-3) and similar <span id="page-116-1"></span>results of Bot et all [\[14\]](#page-172-10) and Geoffrion [\[53\]](#page-176-10) the bi-criteria optimization problem (5.[3\)](#page-115-3) is equivalent to the following parametric optimization problem

$$\begin{cases} \min \left\{ \lambda y - (1 - \lambda) \sum\_{i=1}^{n} p\_i x\_i \right\} \\ \quad \frac{x\_i}{\rho} p\_i \le y, \ i = \overline{1, n} \\ \quad \varepsilon \le x\_i, \ i = \overline{1, n} \\ \quad x\_i \le \rho, \ i = \overline{1, n} \end{cases} \tag{5.4}$$

<span id="page-116-2"></span>with λ ∈ (0, 1) and the following is true

**Lemma 5.4.2** *[\[92\]](#page-179-0)* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R *is an efficient solution for bi-criteria problem* (*[5.3](#page-115-3)*) *if and only if* ∃λ ∈ (0, 1) *such that* (x, y) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R *is an optimal solution for parametric optimization problem* (*[5.4](#page-116-1)*).

The meaning of λ is again the desire of power plant to reduce fluctuation. A big λ means that power plant is focusing on reducing the fluctuation, which might affect turnover, while a small λ means that power plant is focusing more on turnover, accepting a higher fluctuation of energy produced.

**Remark 5.4.3** *Based on the equivalence between problems* [\(5](#page-115-0).2) *and* [\(5](#page-115-3).3) *proved by Lemma [5.4.1](#page-115-1) and the equivalence between* [\(5](#page-115-3).3) *and* (5.[4\)](#page-116-1) *proved by Lemma [5.4.2,](#page-116-2) we state that problems* (5.[2\)](#page-115-0) *and* (5.[4\)](#page-116-1) *are equivalent. Therefore, in order to determine the efficient solution for problem* (5.[2\)](#page-115-0) *it is sufficient to determine the optimal solution for problem* (5.[4\)](#page-116-1)*.*

**Remark 5.4.4** *In the process of computing the optimal solution for parametric optimization problem* [\(5](#page-116-1).4) *we will split the set* {1, 2, ..., n} *in several subsets like* {1, 2, ...l}*,* {l + 1, l + 2, ...m}*,* {m + 1, m + 2, ...t} *and* {t + 1, t + 2, ...n}*. If on such a set or subset the price is constant we will denote it by* p∗*.*

The following *Theorem* presents an optimal solution for the parametric optimization problem [\(5](#page-116-1).4).

### <span id="page-116-0"></span>**Theorem 5.4.5 (Luca and Duca [\[92\]](#page-179-0); parametric index)** *.*

*An optimal solution* (x ∗ , y<sup>∗</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup>×R *for parametric optimization problem* [\(5](#page-116-1).4) *is:*

11. If  $\lambda = \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}$  and  $\frac{\min\_{i=1,n} p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,n} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho'}$  then

$$\left\{ \begin{array}{c} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1, n}} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1, n}} p\_i \right] \end{array} \right\}$$

*else solution does not exist.*

\*\*2.\*\* If  $\lambda > \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}$ , then 
$$\left\{ \begin{array}{c} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p}, \\\\ \hline \end{array} \right.$$

*where* p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

\*\*3.\*\* If  $\lambda < \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}$ , then 
$$\left\{ \begin{array}{c} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p}, \end{array} \right.$$

*where* p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

$$4. \text{ If } \lambda = \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l} \text{ and } \frac{\min\_{i=1,l} p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,l} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho'} \text{ then}$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\\ y^\* = \begin{cases} \overline{p\_2}, & \text{if } \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i = p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ \in \begin{bmatrix} \max\_{j=1, n} p\_j; \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \\ j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{bmatrix}, & \text{if } \max\_{i=1, l}^{\pm} p\_i \le p\_j < \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ \in \begin{bmatrix} \max\_{j=1, l}^{\pm} p\_i; \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \\ \max\_{i=1, l}^{\pm} p\_i; \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \end{bmatrix}, & \text{if } p\_j < \max\_{i=1, l}^{\pm} p\_i, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

*5. If* λ < ρl 1+ρl *, then*

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1}, \; \sharp \overline{\frac{p\_1}{p\_j}} \ge 1, \; j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l*, else solution does not exist.*

## 6. If  $\lambda > \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l}$ , then 
$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_1}, \text{ if } \overline{\frac{p\_1}{p\_j}} \ge \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

p2

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l*, else solution does not exist.*

$$\begin{aligned} \text{7. If } \lambda < \frac{\rho n}{1+\rho n} \text{ and } \frac{\min\_{i=1,l} p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,l} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho'} \text{ then} \\\\ \begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1,l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_2}, \text{ if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=1,l} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \\\ \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases} \end{aligned}$$

*where* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

*8. If* λ > ρl 1+ρl *and* <sup>p</sup><sup>2</sup> p1 = ε ρ *, then* x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ε, i = 1, l x ∗ <sup>j</sup> = ρ, j = l + 1, n y <sup>∗</sup> = ε ρ p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>2</sup>

*where* p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l *and* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n*, else solution does not exist,*

$$\begin{aligned} \text{9. } \text{ If } \lambda > \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n} \text{ and } \frac{\min\_{i=1,l}^{\min p\_i}}{\max\_{i=1,l}^{\text{max}}} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho'} \text{ then} \\\\ \begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1,l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2}, & \text{if} \begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases} \end{aligned}$$

*where* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

$$10. \text{ If } \lambda > \frac{\rho m}{1 + \rho m}, \frac{\min\_i p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,l}} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \text{ and } \frac{\overline{p\_3}}{\overline{p\_2}} = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho'} \text{ then }$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\ x\_k^\* = \rho, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \\ \\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} = \overline{p\_3}, & \text{if} \begin{cases} \max\_{i=1, \overline{l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2} \\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, m *and* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

$$11. \text{ If } \lambda > \frac{\rho l + \rho(n-m)}{1 + \rho l + \rho(n-m)} \text{ and } \frac{\min\_{i=1,l} p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,l} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}, \text{ then }$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ x\_k^\* = \varepsilon, \quad k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, \text{ if } \left\{ \begin{array}{c} \max p\_i \le \overline{p\_3} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \end{array} \right. \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

$$12. \text{ If } \lambda < \frac{\rho l + \rho(n-m)}{1 + \rho l + \rho(n-m)} \text{ and } \frac{\min\_{i=1,l} p\_i}{\max\_{i=1,l} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}, \text{ then }$$

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \\ x\_k^\* = \rho, \quad k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_3}, \; \text{if} \begin{cases} \max\_{i=1, n} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \overline{p\_3} \\\ \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=1, n} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \overline{p\_3}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

\*\*13.\*\* If  $\lambda > \frac{\rho(m-l)}{1+\rho(m-l)}$  and  $\frac{\overline{p\_3}}{\overline{p\_2}} = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}$ , then

$$\begin{cases}
x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\
x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\
x\_k^\* = \rho, \quad k = \overline{m+1,n} \\
y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}\overline{p\_2} = \overline{p\_3}, \text{ if } p\_i \le \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}\overline{p\_2}, \; i = \overline{1,l}
\end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, m *and* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

\*\*14.\*\* If  $\lambda > \frac{\rho l + \rho(t - m)}{1 + \rho l + \rho(t - m)}$ ,  $\frac{\min\_{i=1, l}}{\max\_{i=1, l}} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}$  and  $\frac{\overline{p\_4}}{\overline{p\_3}} = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}$ , then

$$\begin{cases}
x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\
x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\
x\_k^\* = \varepsilon, & k = \overline{m+1, t} \\
x\_s^\* = \rho, & s = \overline{t+1, n}
\end{cases}$$

$$y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\overline{\rho}\overline{p\_3}} = \overline{p\_4}, \text{ if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_3} \\
\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \\
p\_j \le \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, & j = \overline{l+1, m}
\end{cases}$$

*where* p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, t *and* p<sup>4</sup> = ps, s = t + 1, n*, else solution does not exist.*

**Proof.** For a fixed λ ∈ (0, 1), the Lagrangian associated to problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{aligned} L &: \quad \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R} \times \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n \times \mathbb{R}^n \longrightarrow \mathbb{R}, \\ L\left(x, y, a, b, c\right) &= \ \lambda y - (1 - \lambda) \sum\_{i=1}^n p\_i x\_i + \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i \left(x\_i p\_i - \rho y\right) + \rho \\ &+ \sum\_{i=1}^n b\_i \left(\varepsilon - x\_i\right) + \sum\_{i=1}^n c\_i \left(x\_i - \rho\right) \end{aligned}$$

<span id="page-120-1"></span>and the corresponding Kuhn-Tucker conditions are

$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial x\_i} = -\left(1 - \lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i - b\_i + c\_i = 0, \; i = \overline{1, n} \tag{5.5}$$

<span id="page-120-0"></span>
$$\frac{\partial L}{\partial y} = \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^{n} a\_i = 0 \tag{5.6}$$

<span id="page-121-1"></span>
$$\left(p\_i x\_i^\* - \rho y^\*\right) a\_i = 0, \quad a\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{5.7}$$

$$\left(\varepsilon - x\_i^\*\right)b\_i = 0, \quad b\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{5.8}$$

$$\left(x\_i^\* - \rho\right)c\_i = 0, \quad c\_i \ge 0, \quad i = \overline{1, n} \tag{5.9}$$

<span id="page-121-2"></span>where (x ∗ , y<sup>∗</sup> ) ∈ R <sup>n</sup> × R is an optimal solution.

### **Step 1**

<span id="page-121-0"></span>For a fixed i ∈ {1, 2, ...n} the combinations for Kuhn-Tucker multipliers are presented in Table [5.1](#page-121-0)

TABLE 5.1: Combination of KT multipliers for index model

Based on this, we will analyze the existence of solution for each scenario.

### **Scenario 1**

$$a\_i = 0 \mid b\_i = 0 \mid c\_i = 0$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* p\_i \le \rho \boldsymbol{y}^\* \\\ \varepsilon \le \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \\\ \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From last two inequalities we obtain

$$x\_i^\* \in [\varepsilon; \rho]$$

and from first it follows

$$y^\* \ge \frac{x\_i^\*}{\rho} p\_i.$$

Considering that the smallest value for x ∗ i is ε, we get

$$y^\* \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i.$$

Thus, the solution for the system is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* \in [\varepsilon; \rho] \\\ y^\* \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i. \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 2**

$$\begin{array}{|c|c|c|} \hline a\_i > 0 & b\_i = 0 & c\_i = 0 \\ \hline \end{array}$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* p\_i = \rho y^\* \\ \varepsilon \le x\_i^\* \\\ x\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From the first equality it follows

$$x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*.$$

Replacing in the next two inequalities we obtain

$$\begin{cases} \varepsilon \le \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\* \\\ \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\* \le \rho \end{cases}$$

which is equivalent to

$$y^\* \in \left[\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}p\_i; \ p\_i\right].$$

Thus, system's solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\* \\ y^\* \in \left[ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \ p\_i \right] . \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 3**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* p\_i \le \rho \boldsymbol{y}^\* \\\, \varepsilon = \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \\\, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From second equation it follows

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ε.

Third inequality is obvious and replacing in the first one we obtain

$$\varepsilon p\_i \le \rho y^\*$$

equivalent to

$$y^\* \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i$$

and the system has the following solution

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\\, \boldsymbol{y}^\* \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i. \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 4**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* p\_i \le \rho \boldsymbol{y}^\* \\\, \varepsilon \le \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \\\, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* = \rho. \end{cases}$$

From third equality we obtain

$$x\_i^\* = \rho.$$

Second inequality is obvious and replacing in the first one it follows

$$\rho p\_i \le \rho y^\*$$

equivalent to

$$y^\* \ge p\_i$$

which generates the following solution for the system

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho \\\ y^\* \ge p\_i. \end{cases}$$

### **Scenario 5**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> = 0 The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* p\_i = \rho y^\* \\ \varepsilon = x\_i^\* \\ x\_i^\* \le \rho. \end{cases}$$

From second equality it follows

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon.$$

Third inequality is obvious and from the first equality it follows that

$$y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_{i\cdot}$$

In conclusion, system's solution is

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* = \varepsilon \\ \, \boldsymbol{y}^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i. \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 6**

a<sup>i</sup> = 0 b<sup>i</sup> > 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} \, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* p\_i \le \rho \boldsymbol{y}^\* \\\, \varepsilon = \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* \\\, \boldsymbol{x}\_i^\* = \rho. \end{cases}$$

From last two equalities it follows

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

### **Scenario 7**

a<sup>i</sup> > 0 b<sup>i</sup> = 0 c<sup>i</sup> > 0

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* p\_i = \rho y^\* \\ \varepsilon \le x\_i^\* \\\ x\_i^\* = \rho. \end{cases}$$

From last equality it follows

x ∗ <sup>i</sup> = ρ.

Second inequality is obvious and from the first equality we get

$$y^\* = p\_{i\cdot}$$

Thus the corresponding solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho \\ y^\* = p\_i. \end{cases}$$

**Scenario 8**

$$\begin{array}{|c|c|c|} \hline a\_i > 0 & b\_i > 0 & c\_i > 0 \\ \hline \end{array}$$

The system generated by Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.7](#page-121-1)) to ([5.9](#page-121-2)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* p\_i = \rho y^\* \\ \varepsilon = x\_i^\* \\ x\_i^\* = \rho. \end{cases}$$

From last two equalities of the system we obtain

$$x\_i^\* = \varepsilon = \rho$$

which is impossible and thus system has no solution.

Table [5.2](#page-126-0) is presenting the solutions for all possible scenarios.

Completing the analyze for the 8 scenarios, we may conclude that only 6 of them are generating solutions, those being the possible combinations: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 7.

### **Step 2**

At these step we will determine the feasible combinations by analyzing, for i = 1, n, the behavior of possible combinations related to the gradient of Lagrangian.

The system generated by *scenario 1* in relation with [\(5](#page-120-0).5) and [\(5](#page-120-1).6) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\ \lambda = 0 \end{cases}$$

and thus λ = 0 or p<sup>i</sup> = 0, i = 1, n, which is impossible.

<span id="page-126-0"></span>

TABLE 5.2: Possible combinations for index model

The system generated by *scenario 2* in relation with [\(5](#page-120-0).5) and [\(5](#page-120-1).6) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + p\_i a\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i = 0 \end{cases}$$

Then <sup>a</sup><sup>i</sup> = 1 <sup>−</sup> λ, i <sup>=</sup> <sup>1</sup>, n and thus <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = n (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation of the system in follows that

$$
\lambda = \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}.
$$

The system generated by *scenario 3* in relation with ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1 - \lambda\right)p\_i - b\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\ \lambda = 0 \end{cases}$$

It follows that b<sup>i</sup> = − (1 − λ) p<sup>i</sup> < 0, i = 1, n, which is impossible.

The system generated by *scenario 4* in relation with ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + c\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1, n} \\ \lambda = 0 \end{cases}$$

and then c<sup>i</sup> = (1 − λ) p<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, n, which is obvious.

The system generated by *scenario 5* in relation with ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + p\_i a\_i - b\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i = 0 \end{cases}$$

Then b<sup>i</sup> = [a<sup>i</sup> − (1 − λ)] p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n, which has to be positive. It follows that a<sup>i</sup> − (1 − λ) > 0, i = 1, n which means that a<sup>i</sup> > (1 <sup>−</sup> <sup>λ</sup>), i <sup>=</sup> <sup>1</sup>, n and then <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> i=1 a<sup>i</sup> > n (1 − λ). From the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}.
$$

The system generated by *scenario 7* in relation with ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + p\_i a\_i + c\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,n}, \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^n a\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

Then c<sup>i</sup> = [(1 − λ) − a<sup>i</sup> ] p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n, which has to be positive. It follows that (1 − λ) − a<sup>i</sup> > 0, i = 1, n which means that (1 − λ) > ai , i <sup>=</sup> <sup>1</sup>, n and then <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> i=1 a<sup>i</sup> < n (1 − λ). From the last equation of the system it follows that

$$
\lambda < \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}.
$$

<span id="page-127-0"></span>The end results are synthesized in the Table [5.3](#page-127-0)

TABLE 5.3: Behavior of possible combinations related to gradient of Lagrangian for index model

For *scenario 4* we notice that (5.[6\)](#page-120-1) is not satisfied, which means that *scenario 4* will not generate a solution by its own, but may be combined with others to generate solution.

Step 2 of the proof ends with identifying the feasible combinations for the problem. These are 2, 4, 5 and 7.

### **Step 3**

At these step, we identify critical combinations. Analyzing the combining ability for 5 and 7, we notice that prices have to be constant on sets {1, 2, ...l}, respectively {l + 1, l + 2, ...n}. Denoting by p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l and p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, it follows that combination 5 and 7 is not critical if and only if

$$\frac{\overline{p\_2}}{\overline{p\_1}} = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}.$$

Also, for 2 we notice that

$$\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}p\_i \le y^\* \le p\_i, \ i = \overline{1, n}$$

which means that

$$y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1,n}} p\_i \right]$$

and in order that this interval exists it's necessary that

$$\frac{\min\_{i=\overline{1,n}} p\_i}{\max\_{i=\overline{1,n}} p\_i} \ge \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}.$$

### **Step 4**

At this step we will compute an optimal solution for problem [\(5](#page-116-1).4), based on the following combinations:

$$\begin{aligned} &2, 7, 5\\ &2+4, \; 7+4, \; 5+4, \; 2+7, \; 5+7, \; 2+5\\ &\mathbf{2}+\mathbf{5}+7, \; 2+4+5, \; 2+4+7, \; 4+5+7\\ &\mathbf{2}+\mathbf{4}+\mathbf{5}+7 \end{aligned}$$

To compute the optimal solutions we will use the information, stated by Theorem [4.4.11,](#page-102-0) that order of scenarios does not influence the solution.

These combinations are valid only if 5 + 7 is not critical and the interval for 2 exists. If 5 + 7 is critical, then all combinations which contain 5 + 7 have to be eliminated. If the interval for 2 doesn't exist, then all combinations which contain 2 have to be eliminated.

For 2, if λ = ρn 1+ρn and min i=1,n pi max i=1,n pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem (5.[4\)](#page-116-1) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1, n}}^{\underline{\varepsilon}} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1, n}} p\_i \right] \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For *7*, from y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n, we state that all prices are constant on the set {1, 2, ...n}. Denoting by p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n, we obtain that if λ < ρn 1+ρn then an optimal solution for [\(5](#page-116-1).4) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist, where p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n.

For *5*, using a similar principle with 7, we conclude that if λ > ρn 1+ρn then an optimal solution for (5.[4\)](#page-116-1) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, & i = \overline{1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist, where p = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, n.

For combination *2 + 4*, with i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions (5.[5\)](#page-120-0) and (5.[6\)](#page-120-1) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + c\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations, it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ) and replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda = \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , the solution is ∗ ρ ∗

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = 1, l \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \right] \end{cases}$$

and for 4, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

In order to have all conditions for y ∗ fulfilled it is necessary that

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le y^\*, \quad j = \overline{l+1,n} \end{cases}$$

If min i=1,l p<sup>i</sup> < p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, then @ y ∗ .

If min i=1,l p<sup>i</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, then we state that prices are constant on the set {l + 1, l + 2, ...n}, and denoting p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n it follows that

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_2} = \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i.$$

If max i=1,l ε ρ p<sup>i</sup> < p<sup>j</sup> < min i=1,l pi , j = l + 1, n, then y <sup>∗</sup> ∈ max j=l+1,n pj ; min i=1,l pi .

If max i=1,l ε ρ p<sup>i</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, then we state that prices are constant on the set {l + 1, l + 2, ...n}, and denoting p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n it follows that

$$y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{j=l+1,n} p\_j; \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \right].$$

If p<sup>j</sup> < max i=1,l ε ρ pi , j = l + 1, n, then

$$y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \right].$$

As a conclusion, if λ = ρl 1+ρl and min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem (5.[4\)](#page-116-1) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\\ \overline{p\_2} = \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i, & \text{if } \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i = p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \begin{cases} \max\_{j=1, n} p\_j; \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \\\ \varepsilon \left[ \max\_{j=l+1, n} \frac{\varepsilon}{i=1, l} \right], & \text{if } \max\_{i=1, l}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le p\_j < \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ \max\_{i=1, l}^{\varepsilon} p\_i; \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \end{cases} & \text{if } p\_j < \max\_{i=1, l}^{\varepsilon} p\_i, \quad & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ \pm, & \text{if } \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i < p\_j, \quad & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist, where p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n.

For combination *7+ 4*, with i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i + c\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l}, \\\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + c\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations, it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> < l(1 − λ) and replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda < \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l}.
$$

For 7, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ y^\* = p\_i, & i = \overline{1, l} \end{cases}$$

and for 4, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, we conclude that all prices are constant on the set {1, 2, ..., l} . Denoting by p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$
\overline{p\_1} \ge p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n}.
$$

In conclusion, if λ < ρl 1+ρl then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \rho, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_1}, \quad \text{if } \frac{\overline{p\_1}}{p\_j} \ge 1, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *5 + 4*, with i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i - b\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l}, \\\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + c\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations, it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> > l(1 − λ) and replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l}.
$$

For 5, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i, & i = \overline{1, l} \end{cases}$$

and for 4, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, n} .\end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = ε ρ pi , i = 1, l, we conclude that all prices are constant on the set {1, 2, ...l} . Denoting by p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_1} \ge p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n}.$$

In conclusion, if λ > ρl 1+ρl , then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_1}, \quad \text{if } \frac{\overline{p\_1}}{p\_j} \ge \frac{\varrho}{\varepsilon}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *2 + 7*, with i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + a\_j p\_j + c\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^n a\_j = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ). From the next set of *n-l* equations it follows that <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> j=l+1 a<sup>j</sup> < (n − l) (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda < \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ then the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=1,l} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \right] \end{cases}$$

and for 7, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, we conclude that all prices are constant on the set {l + 1, l + 2, ...n} . Denoting by p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_2} \end{cases}$$

which implies that

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \overline{p\_2} \\\ \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \end{cases}$$

As a conclusion, if λ < ρn 1+ρn and min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_2}, & \text{if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=1, l} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \overline{p\_2} \\\ \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *5 + 7*, with i = 1, l, j = l + 1, n, if non-critical condition is fulfilled (p<sup>1</sup> = p<sup>i</sup> , i = 1, l, p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n and p2 p1 = ε ρ ), then Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)\overline{p\_1} + a\_i\overline{p\_1} - b\_i = 0, & i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}\overline{p\_1} + a\_j\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}\overline{p\_1} + c\_j = 0, & j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho\sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho\sum\_{j=l+1}^n a\_j = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> > l(1 − λ). From the next set of *n-l* equations it follows that

<span id="page-134-0"></span>
$$\sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} a\_j < \left(n-l\right)\left(1-\lambda\right). \tag{5.10}$$

Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda - \rho l \left( 1 - \lambda \right) - \rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} a\_j > 0
$$

$$
\rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} a\_j < \lambda - \rho l \left(1 - \lambda\right). \tag{5.11}
$$

<span id="page-135-1"></span>Because ∃ j ∈ {l + 1, l + 2, ..., n} such that a<sup>j</sup> > 0 it follows that

<span id="page-135-0"></span>
$$\sum\_{j=l+1}^{n} a\_j > 0.\tag{5.12}$$

From ([5.10](#page-134-0)) and ([5.12](#page-135-0)) it follows that (n − l) (1 − λ) > 0, which is obvious.

From ([5.11](#page-135-1)) and ([5.12](#page-135-0)) it follows that λ − ρl(1 − λ) > 0, which leads to

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho l}{1 + \rho l}.
$$

For 5, with i = 1, l the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_1} \end{cases}$$

and for 7, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} \; x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1,n},\\ \; y^\* = \overline{p\_2}. \end{cases}$$

It follows that if λ > ρl 1+ρl and non-critical condition is fulfilled, then an optimal solution for the problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \varepsilon, & i = \overline{1, l} \\ x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_1} = \overline{p\_2} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *2 + 5*, with i = 1, l and j = l + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + a\_j p\_j - b\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,n} \\\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^n a\_j = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ). From the next set of *n-l* equations it follows that <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> j=l+1 a<sup>j</sup> > (n − l) (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho n}{1 + \rho n}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \right] \end{cases}$$

and for 5, with j = l + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = ε ρ pj , j = l + 1, n, we conclude that all prices are constant on the set {l + 1, l + 2, ...n} . Denoting by p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, n, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \end{cases}$$

which implies that

$$\max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2}$$

$$\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho}\overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i.$$

If λ > ρn 1+ρn and min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, & j = \overline{l+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2}, & \text{if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *2 + 5 + 7*, with i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m and k = m + 1, n, if non-critical condition is fulfilled (p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, m; p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n and <sup>p</sup><sup>3</sup> p2 = ε ρ ), then Kuhn-Tucker conditions [\(5](#page-120-0).5) and (5.[6\)](#page-120-1) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)\overline{p\_2} + a\_j \overline{p\_2} - b\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)\overline{p\_3} + a\_k \overline{p\_3} + c\_k = 0, \quad k = \overline{m+1,n} \\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho \sum\_{j=l+1}^m a\_j - \rho \sum\_{k=m+1}^n a\_k = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ). From the next set of *m-l* equations it follows that

<span id="page-137-0"></span>
$$\sum\_{j=l+1}^{m} a\_j > (m-l)\left(1-\lambda\right).$$

From the next set of *n-m* equations it follows that

<span id="page-137-2"></span>
$$\sum\_{k=m+1}^{n} a\_k < \left(n-m\right)\left(1-\lambda\right). \tag{5.13}$$

Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$\rho \sum\_{k=m+1}^{n} a\_k < \lambda - \rho l \left(1 - \lambda\right) - \rho \left(m - l\right) \left(1 - \lambda\right). \tag{5.14}$$

Because ∃ k ∈ {m + 1, m + 2, ..., n} such that a<sup>k</sup> > 0 it follows that

<span id="page-137-1"></span>
$$\sum\_{k=m+1}^{n} a\_k > 0.\tag{5.15}$$

From (5.[13\)](#page-137-0) and (5.[15\)](#page-137-1) it follows that (n − m) (1 − λ) > 0, which is obvious.

From (5.[14\)](#page-137-2) and (5.[15\)](#page-137-1) it follows that λ−ρl(1 − λ)−ρ (m − l) (1 − λ) > 0, which leads to

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho m}{1 + \rho m}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}}^{\varepsilon} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \right] \end{cases}$$

for 5, with j = l + 1, m the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \end{cases}$$

and for 7, with k = m + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & k = \overline{m+1,n} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_3}. \end{cases}$$

Under this assumptions, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

∗

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=1,l}^{\underline{\varepsilon}} p\_i \le y \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \\\ y^\* = \frac{\underline{\varepsilon}}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_3} \end{cases}$$

which implies that

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2} \\\frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ \overline{p\_3} = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} .\end{cases}$$

If λ > ρm 1+ρm , min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ and non-critical condition is fulfilled, then an optimal solution for problem [\(5](#page-116-1).4) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \varepsilon, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ x\_k^\* = \rho, \quad k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} = \overline{p\_3}, \text{ if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=1, \overline{l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_2} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_2} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist, where p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, m and p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n.

For combination *2 + 4 + 5*, with i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m and k = m + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + c\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_k + a\_k p\_k - b\_k = 0, \quad k = \overline{m+1,n} \\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho \sum\_{k=m+1}^n a\_k = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ). From the next set of *m-l* equations it follows that c<sup>j</sup> = (1 − λ) p<sup>j</sup> > 0, j = l + 1, m, which is obvious. From the next set of *n–m* equations it follows that <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup>

k=m+1 a<sup>k</sup> > (n − m) (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda > \frac{\rho l + \rho \,(n - m)}{1 + \rho l + \rho \,(n - m)}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then the solution is

$$\left\{ \begin{array}{l} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}}^{\underline{\varepsilon}} p\_i; \min\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \right] .\end{array} \right\}$$

For 4, with j = l + 1, m the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, m}. \end{cases}$$

For 5, with k = m + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_k^\* = \varepsilon, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_k, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = ε ρ pk, k = m + 1, n, we state that all prices are constant on {m + 1, m + 2, ...n} . Denoting by p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3} \end{cases}$$

which implies

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_3} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m}. \end{cases}$$

In conclusion, if λ > ρl+ρ(n−m) 1+ρl+ρ(n−m) and min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ x\_k^\* = \varepsilon, \quad k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ y^\* = \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, & \text{if } \begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1, l}} p\_i \le \overline{p\_3} \\\ \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=1, l} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} \overline{p\_3}, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \end{cases} \end{cases}$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *2 + 4 + 7*, with i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m, k = m + 1, n, the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be

$$\begin{cases} -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_i + a\_i p\_i = 0, \quad i = \overline{1,l} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_j + c\_j = 0, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\ -\left(1-\lambda\right)p\_k + a\_k p\_k + c\_k = 0, \quad k = \overline{m+1,n} \\ \lambda - \rho \sum\_{i=1}^l a\_i - \rho \sum\_{k=m+1}^n a\_k = 0. \end{cases}$$

From the first set of *l* equations it follows that P l i=1 a<sup>i</sup> = l(1 − λ). From the next set of *m-l* equations it follows that c<sup>j</sup> = (1 − λ) p<sup>j</sup> > 0, j = l + 1, m, which is obvious.

From the next set of *n–m* equations it follows that <sup>P</sup><sup>n</sup> k=m+1 a<sup>k</sup> < (n − m) (1 − λ). Replacing in the last equation we obtain

$$
\lambda < \frac{\rho l + \rho \left(n - m\right)}{1 + \rho l + \rho \left(n - m\right)}.
$$

For 2, with i = 1, l, if min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then the solution is

$$\left\{ \begin{array}{l} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, \quad i = \overline{1, l} \\\ y^\* \in \left[ \max\limits\_{i=\overline{1,l}} \frac{\varepsilon}{\rho} p\_i; \min\limits\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \right] .\end{array} \right\}.$$

For 4, with j = l + 1, m the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_j^\* = \rho, & j = \overline{l+1, m} \\ y^\* \ge p\_j, & j = \overline{l+1, m}. \end{cases}$$

For 7, with k = m + 1, n the solution is

$$\begin{cases} x\_k^\* = \rho, & k = \overline{m+1, n} \\ y^\* = p\_k, & k = \overline{m+1, n}. \end{cases}$$

From y <sup>∗</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n, we state that all prices are constant on {m + 1, m + 2, ...n} . Denoting by p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n, the conditions which have to be fulfilled by y <sup>∗</sup> are

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}}^{\varepsilon} p\_i \le y^\* \\\ y^\* \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ y^\* \ge p\_j, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m} \\\ y^\* = \overline{p\_3} \end{cases}$$

which implies

$$\begin{cases} \max\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \overline{p\_3} \\\ \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=\overline{1,l}} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \overline{p\_3}, \quad j = \overline{l+1,m}. \end{cases}$$

If λ < ρl+ρ(n−m) 1+ρl+ρ(n−m) and min i=1,l pi max i=1,l pi ≥ ε ρ , then an optimal solution for problem ([5.4](#page-116-1)) is

$$\begin{cases} x\_i^\* = \frac{\rho}{p\_i} y^\*, & i = \overline{1, l} \\\ x\_j^\* = \rho, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \\\ x\_k^\* = \rho, \quad k = \overline{m+1, n} \\\ \end{cases}$$

$$y^\* = \overline{p\_3}, \text{ if } \left\{ \begin{array}{l} \max\_{i=1,l} p\_i \le \frac{\rho}{\varepsilon} \overline{p\_3} \\\ \overline{p\_3} \le \min\_{i=1,l} p\_i \\\ p\_j \le \overline{p\_3}, \quad j = \overline{l+1, m} \end{array} \right.$$

else solution does not exist.

For combination *4 + 5 + 7*, with i = 1, l, j = l + 1, m and k = m + 1, n, if non-critical condition is fulfilled (p<sup>2</sup> = p<sup>j</sup> , j = l + 1, m, p<sup>3</sup> = pk, k = m + 1, n and <sup>p</sup><sup>3</sup> p2 = ε ρ ), the Kuhn-Tucker conditions ([5.5](#page-120-0)) and ([5.6](#page-120-1)) will be
